<p><i>Falco gentilis</i> <span style="font-size:85%;">Linnaeus,&#160;1758</span>
</p>

</tbody></table>
<p>The <b>northern goshawk</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ɡ/: &#39;g&#39; in &#39;guy&#39;">ɡ</span><span title="/ɒ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;body&#39;">ɒ</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="&#39;h&#39; in &#39;hi&#39;">h</span><span title="/ɔː/: &#39;au&#39; in &#39;fraud&#39;">ɔː</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <i>Accipiter gentilis</i>) is a medium-large <a href="/wiki/Bird_of_prey" title="Bird of prey">raptor</a> in the family <a href="/wiki/Accipitridae" title="Accipitridae">Accipitridae</a>, which also includes other extant diurnal raptors, such as <a href="/wiki/Eagle" title="Eagle">eagles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buzzard" title="Buzzard">buzzards</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harrier_(bird)" title="Harrier (bird)">harriers</a>. As a species in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Accipiter" title="Accipiter">Accipiter</a></i>, the goshawk is often considered a "true hawk".<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The scientific name is <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>; <i>Accipiter</i> is "hawk", from <i>accipere</i>, "to grasp", and <i>gentilis</i> is "noble" or "gentle" because in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> only the nobility were permitted to fly goshawks for <a href="/wiki/Falconry" title="Falconry">falconry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jobling_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jobling-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This species was first described under its current scientific name by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Linnaeus</a> in his <i>Systema naturae</i> in <a href="/wiki/10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="10th edition of Systema Naturae">1758</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Linnaeus1758_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Linnaeus1758-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It is a widespread species that inhabits many of the temperate parts of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a>. The northern goshawk is the only species in the genus <i>Accipiter</i> found in both <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> It may have the second widest distribution of any true member of the family <a href="/wiki/Accipitridae" title="Accipitridae">Accipitridae</a>, behind arguably only the <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagle</a> (<i>Aquila chrysaetos</i>), which has a broader range to the south of Asia than the goshawk. The only other acciptrid species to also range in both North America and Eurasia according to  current opinion, is the more <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a>-restricted <a href="/wiki/Rough-legged_buzzard" title="Rough-legged buzzard">rough-legged buzzard</a> (<i>Buteo lagopus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Except in a small portion of southern <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, it is the only species of "goshawk" in its range and it is thus often referred to, both officially and unofficially, as simply the "goshawk". It is mainly <a href="/wiki/Resident_bird" class="mw-redirect" title="Resident bird">resident</a>, but birds from colder regions <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migrate</a> south for the winter.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In North America, migratory goshawks are often seen migrating south along <a href="/wiki/Ridge" title="Ridge">mountain ridge</a> tops at nearly any time of the fall depending on latitude.<sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution">Distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The northern goshawk has a large circumpolar distribution. In <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a>, it is found in most areas of Europe excluding <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>. It has a fairly spotty distribution in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">western Europe</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>) but is more or less found continuously through the rest of the continent. Their Eurasian distribution sweeps continuously across most of Russia, excluding the fully treeless <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a> in the northern stretches, to the western limits of <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> as far as <a href="/wiki/Anadyr_(town)" title="Anadyr (town)">Anadyr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kamchatka" class="mw-redirect" title="Kamchatka">Kamchatka</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Hemisphere" title="Eastern Hemisphere">Eastern Hemisphere</a>, they are found in their southern limits in extreme northwestern <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corsica" title="Corsica">Corsica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">Sardinia</a>, the "toe" of Italy, southern Greece, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sinkiang" class="mw-redirect" title="Sinkiang">Sinkiang</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Tien_Shan" class="mw-redirect" title="Tien Shan">Tien Shan</a>, in some parts of <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a>, western China and Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In winter, northern goshawks may be found rarely as far south as <a href="/wiki/Taif" class="mw-redirect" title="Taif">Taif</a> in <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> and perhaps <a href="/wiki/Tonkin" title="Tonkin">Tonkin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, they are most broadly found in the <a href="/wiki/Western_United_States" title="Western United States">western United States</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Western_Canada" title="Western Canada">western Canada</a>. Their breeding range in the western contiguous United States largely consists of the wooded foothills of the <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains" title="Rocky Mountains">Rocky Mountains</a> and many other large mountain ranges from <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a> to <a href="/wiki/Southern_California" title="Southern California">southern California</a> extending east to central <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a> and westernmost <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Somewhat discontinuous breeding populations are found in southeastern <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a> and southwestern <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>, thence also somewhat spottily into western Mexico down through <a href="/wiki/Sonora" title="Sonora">Sonora</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chihuahua_City" title="Chihuahua City">Chihuahua</a> along the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Madre_Occidental" title="Sierra Madre Occidental">Sierra Madre Occidental</a> as far as <a href="/wiki/Jalisco" title="Jalisco">Jalisco</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guerrero" title="Guerrero">Guerrero</a>, their worldwide southern limit as a breeding species.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The goshawk continues east through much of <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> as a native species, but is rarer in most of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_United_States" title="Eastern United States">eastern United States</a>, especially the <a href="/wiki/Midwestern_United_States" title="Midwestern United States">Midwest</a> where they are not typically found outside the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a> region, where a good-sized breeding population occurs in the northern parts of <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a> and somewhat into <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a>; a very small population persists in the extreme northeast corner of <a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">North Dakota</a>. They breed also in mountainous areas of <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>, central <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a> and northwestern <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>, sporadically down to extreme northwestern <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a> and northeastern <a href="/wiki/West_Virginia" title="West Virginia">West Virginia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Vagrants have been reported in Ireland, <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> (central Morocco, northern <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>); the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> (<a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, Saudi Arabia), <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Asia" class="mw-redirect" title="Southwest Asia">southwest Asia</a> (southern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>), western India (<a href="/wiki/Gujarat" title="Gujarat">Gujarat</a>) and on <a href="/wiki/Izu_Islands" title="Izu Islands">Izu-shoto</a> (south of Japan) and the <a href="/wiki/Commander_Islands" title="Commander Islands">Commander Islands</a>, and in most of the parts of the United States where they do not breed.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitat">Habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_gentilis_Kaibab.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Accipiter_gentilis_Kaibab.jpg/220px-Accipiter_gentilis_Kaibab.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Accipiter_gentilis_Kaibab.jpg/330px-Accipiter_gentilis_Kaibab.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Accipiter_gentilis_Kaibab.jpg/440px-Accipiter_gentilis_Kaibab.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1862" data-file-height="1247" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_gentilis_Kaibab.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Adult in the <a href="/wiki/Kaibab_Plateau" title="Kaibab Plateau">Kaibab Plateau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>, in a <a href="/wiki/Pine" title="Pine">pine</a> tree that typifies the habitat used locally</div></div></div>
<p>Northern goshawks can be found in both <a href="/wiki/Deciduous" title="Deciduous">deciduous</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coniferous" class="mw-redirect" title="Coniferous">coniferous</a> forests. While the species might show strong regional preferences for certain trees, they seem to have no strong overall preferences nor even a preference between deciduous or coniferous trees despite claims to the contrary.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Penteriani,_V._2002_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penteriani,_V._2002-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> More important than the type of trees are the composition of a given tree stand, which should be tall, <a href="/wiki/Old-growth_forest" title="Old-growth forest">old-growth</a> with intermediate to heavy canopy coverage (often more than 40%) and minimal density undergrowth, both of which are favorable for hunting conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Also, goshawks typically require close proximity to <a href="/wiki/Woodland_edge" title="Woodland edge">openings</a> in which to execute additional hunting.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> More so than in <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, the goshawks of Eurasia, especially <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">central Europe</a>, may live in fairly urbanized patchworks of small woods, <a href="/wiki/Windbreak" title="Windbreak">shelter-belts</a> and copses and even use largely isolated trees in <a href="/wiki/Peri-urbanisation" title="Peri-urbanisation">central parts of Eurasian cities</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bednarek_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bednarek-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Link_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Link-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Access to <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">waterways</a> and <a href="/wiki/Riparian_zone" title="Riparian zone">riparian zones</a> of any kind is not uncommon in goshawk home ranges but seems to not be a requirement.<sup id="cite_ref-Bull_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bull-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Narrow tree-lined riparian zones in otherwise relatively open habitats can provide suitable wintering habitat in the absence of more extensive woodlands.<sup id="cite_ref-Squires_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Squires-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The northern goshawk can be found at almost any altitude, but recently is typically found at <a href="/wiki/Montane_forest" class="mw-redirect" title="Montane forest">high elevations</a> due to a paucity of extensive forests remaining in lowlands across much of its range. Altitudinally, goshawks may live anywhere up to a given mountain range's <a href="/wiki/Tree_line" title="Tree line">tree line</a>, which is usually 3,000&#160;m (9,800&#160;ft) in elevation or less.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The northern limit of their distribution also coincides with the tree line and here may adapt to <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_forest" title="Dwarf forest">dwarf tree communities</a>, often along drainages of the lower <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Swem_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swem-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Englemann_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Englemann-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> In winter months, the northernmost or high mountain populations move down to warmer forests with lower elevations, often continuing to avoid detection except while migrating. A majority of goshawks around the world remain sedentary throughout the year.<sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_gentilisAAP045CA.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Accipiter_gentilisAAP045CA.jpg/220px-Accipiter_gentilisAAP045CA.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="205" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Accipiter_gentilisAAP045CA.jpg/330px-Accipiter_gentilisAAP045CA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Accipiter_gentilisAAP045CA.jpg/440px-Accipiter_gentilisAAP045CA.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="955" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_gentilisAAP045CA.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Juvenile (left) and adult by <a href="/wiki/Louis_Agassiz_Fuertes" title="Louis Agassiz Fuertes">Louis Agassiz Fuertes</a></div></div></div>
<p>The northern goshawk has relatively short, broad wings and a long tail, typical for <i>Accipiter</i> species and common to raptors that require maneuverability within forest habitats.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> For an <i>Accipiter</i>, it has a relatively sizeable bill, relatively long wings, a relatively short tail, robust and fairly short legs and particularly thick toes.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Across most of the species' range, it is blue-grey above or brownish-grey with dark barring or streaking over a grey or white base color below, but Asian subspecies in particular range from nearly white overall to nearly black above.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Goshawks tend to show <a href="/wiki/Cline_(biology)" title="Cline (biology)">clinal</a> variation in color, with most goshawks further north being paler and those in warmer areas being darker but individuals can be either dark in the north or pale in the south. Individuals that live a long life may gradually become paler as they age, manifesting in mottling and a lightening of the back from a darker shade to a bluer pale color.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Its plumage is more variable than that of the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_sparrowhawk" title="Eurasian sparrowhawk">Eurasian sparrowhawk</a> (<i>Accipiter nisus</i>), which is probably due to higher genetic variability in the larger goshawk.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The juvenile northern goshawk is usually a solid to mildly streaky brown above, with many variations in underside color from nearly pure white to almost entirely overlaid with broad dark cinnamon-brown striping. Both juveniles and adults have a barred tail, with 3 to 5 dark brown or black bars.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Adults always have a white eye stripe or <a href="/wiki/Supercilia" class="mw-redirect" title="Supercilia">supercilia</a>, which tends to be broader in northern Eurasian and North American birds.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In North America, juveniles have pale-yellow eyes, and adults develop dark red eyes usually after their second year, although nutrition and genetics may affect eye color as well.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> In Europe and Asia, juveniles also have pale-yellow eyes while adults typically develop orange-colored eyes, though some may have only brighter yellow or occasionally ochre or brownish eye color.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Moulting" title="Moulting">Moulting</a> starts between late March and late May, the male tends to moult later and faster than the female. Moulting results in the female being especially likely to have a gap in its wing feathers while incubating and this may cause some risk, especially if the male is lost, as it inhibits her hunting abilities and may hamper her defensive capabilities, putting both herself and the nestlings in potential danger of predation. The moult takes a total of 4–6 months, with tail feathers following the wings then lastly the <a href="/wiki/Feather_development" title="Feather development">contour and body feathers</a>, which may not be completely moulted even as late as October.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although existing wing size and body mass measurements indicate that the <a href="/wiki/Henst%27s_goshawk" title="Henst&#39;s goshawk">Henst's goshawk</a> (<i>Accipiter henstii</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Meyer%27s_goshawk" title="Meyer&#39;s goshawk">Meyer's goshawk</a> (<i>Accipiter meyerianus</i>) broadly overlap in size with this species, the northern goshawk is on average the largest member of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Accipiter" title="Accipiter">Accipiter</a></i>, especially outsizing its tropic cousins in the larger Eurasian races.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BirdsOfQuebec_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BirdsOfQuebec-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The northern goshawk, like all <i>Accipiters</i>, exhibits <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexual dimorphism</a>, where females are significantly larger than males, with the dimorphism notably greater in most parts of Eurasia. Linearly, males average about 8% smaller in North America and 13% smaller than females in Eurasia, but in the latter landmass can range up to a very noticeable 28% difference in extreme cases.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Male northern goshawks are 46 to 61&#160;cm (18 to 24&#160;in) long and have a 89 to 105&#160;cm (35 to 41&#160;in) wingspan. The female is much larger, 58 to 69&#160;cm (23 to 27&#160;in) long with a 108 to 127&#160;cm (43 to 50&#160;in) wingspan.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In a study of North American goshawks (<i>A. g. atricapillus</i>), males were found to average 56&#160;cm (22&#160;in) in total length, against females which averaged 61&#160;cm (24&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Males from six subspecies average around 762&#160;g (1.680&#160;lb) in body mass, with a range from all races of 357 to 1,200&#160;g (0.787 to 2.646&#160;lb). The female can be up to more than twice as heavy, averaging from the same races 1,150&#160;g (2.54&#160;lb) with an overall range of 758 to 2,200&#160;g (1.671 to 4.850&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Among standard measurements, the most oft-measured is <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">wing chord</a> which can range from 286 to 354&#160;mm (11.3 to 13.9&#160;in) in males and from 324 to 390&#160;mm (12.8 to 15.4&#160;in) in females. Additional, the tail is 200–295&#160;mm (7.9–11.6&#160;in), the <a href="/wiki/Culmen_(bird)" class="mw-redirect" title="Culmen (bird)">culmen</a> is 20–26.3&#160;mm (0.79–1.04&#160;in) and the <a href="/wiki/Tarsus_(skeleton)" title="Tarsus (skeleton)">tarsus</a> is 68–90&#160;mm (2.7–3.5&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnson1989_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson1989-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-indianbiodiversity.org_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-indianbiodiversity.org-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Voice">Voice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Voice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_1" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="5.4" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Accipiter_gentilis_-_Northern_Goshawk_XC124805.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/3/3c/Accipiter_gentilis_-_Northern_Goshawk_XC124805.ogg/Accipiter_gentilis_-_Northern_Goshawk_XC124805.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="122864" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3c/Accipiter_gentilis_-_Northern_Goshawk_XC124805.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (206 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="205930" /></audio></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_gentilis_-_Northern_Goshawk_XC124805.ogg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Accipiter gentilis</i> – northern goshawk</div></div></div>
<p>Northern goshawks normally only vocalize during courtship or the nesting season. Adult goshawks may chatter a repeated note, varying in speed and volume based on the context. When calling from a perch, birds often turn their heads slowly from side to side, producing a ventriloquial effect.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The male calls a fast, high-pitched <i>kew-kew-kew</i> when delivering food or else a very different croaking <i>guck</i> or <i>chup</i>. The latter sound has been considered by some authors similar to that of a person snapping the tongue away from the roof the mouth; the males produce it by holding the beak wide open, thrusting the head up and forward, than bringing it down as the sound is emitted, repeated at intervals of five seconds. This call is uttered when the male encounters a female.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Two calls have been recorded mainly from brooding females in the race <i>A. g. atricapillus</i>: a recognition scream of short, intense notes (<i>whee-o</i> or <i>hee-ya</i>) which ends in harsh, falsetto tone; then a dismissal call given when the male lingers after delivering food, consisting of a choked, cut-off scream.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, the adult female's rapid strident <i>kek-kek-kek</i> expresses alarm or intent to mob towards threatening intruders. This is often done when mobbing a predator such as a <a href="/wiki/Great_horned_owl" title="Great horned owl">great horned owl</a> (<i>Bubo virginianus</i>) and as it progresses the female's voice may lower slightly in pitch and becomes harsh and rasping. As the intensity of her attacks increases, her kakking becomes more rapid and can attain a constant screaming quality. Females often withdraw into the treetops when fatigued, and their calls are then spaced at longer intervals. Males respond to interlopers or predators with a quieter, slower <i>gek gek gek</i> or <i>ep ep ep</i>. A call consisting of <i>kek…kek.kekk kek kek-kek-kek</i> is used mainly by females in advertisement and during pre-laying mutual calling. Both sexes also may engage in kakking during copulation.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schnell_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schnell-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Vocalizations mainly peak in late courtship/early nesting around late March to April, can begin up to 45 minutes before sunrise, and are more than twice in as frequent in the first three hours of daylight as in the rest of the day.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Occasionally hunting northern goshawks may make shrill screams when pursuing prey, especially if a lengthy chase is undertaken and the prey is already aware of its intended predator.<sup id="cite_ref-Zirrer_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zirrer-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Taxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The genus <i>Accipiter</i> contains nearly 50 known living species and is the most diverse genus of diurnal raptors in the world. This group of agile, smallish, forest-dwelling hawks has been in existence for possibly tens of millions of years, probably as an adaptation to the explosive numbers of small birds that began to occupy the world's forest in the last few eras. The <a href="/wiki/Harrier_(bird)" title="Harrier (bird)">harriers</a> are the only group of extant diurnal raptors that seem to bear remotely close relation to this genus, whereas <a href="/wiki/Buteoninae" title="Buteoninae">buteonines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Milvinae" title="Milvinae">Old World kites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sea_eagle" title="Sea eagle">sea eagles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Melierax" title="Melierax">chanting-goshawks</a> are much more distantly related and all other modern accipitrids are not directly related.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Within the genus <i>Accipiter</i>, the northern goshawk seems to belong to a <a href="/wiki/Species_complex" title="Species complex">superspecies</a> with other larger goshawks from different portions of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Meyer%27s_goshawk" title="Meyer&#39;s goshawk">Meyer's goshawk</a>, found in the <a href="/wiki/South_Pacific_Ocean" class="mw-redirect" title="South Pacific Ocean">South Pacific</a>, has been posited as the most likely to be most close related living cousin to the northern goshawk, the somewhat puzzling gap in their respective ranges explained by other <a href="/wiki/Palearctic" class="mw-redirect" title="Palearctic">Palearctic</a> raptors such as <a href="/wiki/Bonelli%27s_eagle" title="Bonelli&#39;s eagle">Bonelli's eagles</a> (<i>Aquila fasciata</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Short-toed_eagle" class="mw-redirect" title="Short-toed eagle">short-toed eagles</a> (<i>Circaetus gallicus</i>) that have extant isolated tropical island populations and were probably part of the same southwest Pacific radiation that led to the Meyer's goshawk.<sup id="cite_ref-Wattel_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wattel-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> A presumably older radiation of this group occurred in Africa, where it led to both the <a href="/wiki/Henst%27s_goshawk" title="Henst&#39;s goshawk">Henst's goshawk</a> of <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Black_sparrowhawk" title="Black sparrowhawk">black sparrowhawk</a> (<i>Accipiter melanoleucus</i>) of the mainland. While the Henst's goshawk quite resembles the northern goshawks, the black sparrowhawk is superficially described as a “sparrowhawk” due to its relatively much longer and finer legs than those of typical goshawks but overall its size and plumage (especially that of juveniles) is much more goshawk than sparrowhawk-like.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wattel_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wattel-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Outside of the presumed superspecies, the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Erythrotriorchis" title="Erythrotriorchis">Erythrotriorchis</a></i> may be part of an Australasian radiation of basal goshawks based largely on their similar morphology to northern goshawks.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Genetic studies have indicated that the <a href="/wiki/Cooper%27s_hawk" title="Cooper&#39;s hawk">Cooper's hawk</a> of North America is also fairly closely related to the northern goshawk, having been present in North America before either of the other two North American <i>Accipiters</i>. However, the much smaller sharp-shinned hawk, which has <a href="/wiki/Covergent_evolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Covergent evolution">similar plumage</a> to the Cooper's hawk and seems to be most closely related to the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_sparrowhawk" title="Eurasian sparrowhawk">Eurasian sparrowhawk</a>, appears to have occupied North America the latest of the three North American species, despite having the broadest current distribution of any <i>Accipiter</i> in the Americas (extending down through much of <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The term <i>goshawk</i> comes from the <a href="/wiki/Old_English_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Old English language">Old English</a> <i>gōsheafoc</i>, "goose-hawk".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subspecies">Subspecies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Subspecies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_gentilis,_Hamburg,_Germany.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Accipiter_gentilis%2C_Hamburg%2C_Germany.png/220px-Accipiter_gentilis%2C_Hamburg%2C_Germany.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Accipiter_gentilis%2C_Hamburg%2C_Germany.png/330px-Accipiter_gentilis%2C_Hamburg%2C_Germany.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Accipiter_gentilis%2C_Hamburg%2C_Germany.png/440px-Accipiter_gentilis%2C_Hamburg%2C_Germany.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="598" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_gentilis,_Hamburg,_Germany.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Typical adult with a strong brownish-gray cast, from the nominate subspecies, <i>A. g. gentilis</i></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Goshawkmale66.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Goshawkmale66.jpg/220px-Goshawkmale66.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Goshawkmale66.jpg/330px-Goshawkmale66.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Goshawkmale66.jpg/440px-Goshawkmale66.jpg 2x" data-file-width="856" data-file-height="574" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Goshawkmale66.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Typical adult from the American subspecies (<i>A. g. atricapillus</i>) showing its strong supercilium, red eyes, black head, and blue-gray back</div></div></div>
<p>The northern goshawk appears to have diversified in northern, central Eurasia and spread both westwards to occupy Europe and, later on, eastwards to spread into <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> across the <a href="/wiki/Bering_Land_Bridge" class="mw-redirect" title="Bering Land Bridge">Bering Land Bridge</a>. Fossil remains show that goshawks were present in <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene era</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Two non-exclusive processes could have occurred to cause the notably color and size variation of northern goshawks throughout its range: isolation in the past enabled gene combinations to assort as distinct morphs that suited conditions in different geographical areas, followed by a remixing of these genotypes to result in clines, or subtle variation in modern selection pressures led to a diversity of hues and patterns.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Palmer_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palmer-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> As a result of the high variation of individual goshawks in plumage characteristics and typical trends in clinal variation and size variations that largely follow <a href="/wiki/Bergmann%27s_rule" title="Bergmann&#39;s rule">Bergmann's rule</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gloger%27s_rule" title="Gloger&#39;s rule">Gloger's rule</a>, an excessive number of subspecies have been described for the northern goshawk in the past. In Europe (including <a href="/wiki/European_Russia" title="European Russia">European Russia</a>) alone, 12 subspecies were described between 1758 and 1990.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gladkov_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gladkov-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Most modern authorities agree on listing nine to ten subspecies of northern goshawks from throughout its range.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><span class="anchor" id="Accipiter_gentilis_gentilis"></span> <i>A. g. gentilis</i> (Linnaeus, 1758) – The nominate race is distributed through most of the species current European range, excluding northern <a href="/wiki/Fennoscandia" title="Fennoscandia">Fennoscandia</a>, northwestern Russia and possibly some of the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_island" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean island">Mediterranean islands</a> they inhabit. Outside of Europe, this subspecies' range extends south to <a href="/wiki/Northwestern_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Northwestern Africa">northwestern Africa</a> (almost entirely <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>) and east in Eurasia to <a href="/wiki/Ural_Mountain" class="mw-redirect" title="Ural Mountain">Urals</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>. It is a typically large subspecies, with high levels of sexual dimorphism. The <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">wing chord</a> of males ranges from 300 to 342&#160;mm (11.8 to 13.5&#160;in) and of females from 336 to 385&#160;mm (13.2 to 15.2&#160;in). Body mass is variable, range from 517 to 1,110&#160;g (1.140 to 2.447&#160;lb) in males and from 820 to 2,200&#160;g (1.81 to 4.85&#160;lb) in females. In some cases, the largest adult females (including some exceptionally big females which are the heaviest goshawks known from anywhere) from within a population are up to four times heavier than the smallest adult males, although this is exceptional.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The highest average weights come from central <a href="/wiki/Fennoscandia" title="Fennoscandia">Fennoscandia</a>, where the sexes weigh on average 865&#160;g (1.907&#160;lb) and 1,414&#160;g (3.117&#160;lb), respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The lowest come from Spain, where goshawks of this race weigh a median of 690&#160;g (1.52&#160;lb) in males and 1,050&#160;g (2.31&#160;lb) in females.<sup id="cite_ref-Zuberogoitia_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zuberogoitia-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The nominate race is generally a dark slaty-brown color on its back and wing coverts with a blackish-brown head. The supercilium is thin and the underside is generally creamy with heavy dark barring. On average, in addition to their smaller size, nominate goshawks to the south of the race's distribution have thinner supericilia and broader and denser barring on the underside.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gladkov_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gladkov-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> An aberrant “<a href="/wiki/Isabelline_(colour)" title="Isabelline (colour)">isabelline</a>” morph is known mainly from <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">central</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">eastern Europe</a>, where the goshawk may be a general beige color (somewhat similar to the pale birds from the races <i>albidus</i> and <i>buteoides</i>), but such birds appear to be very rare.<sup id="cite_ref-Fischer_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="anchor" id="Accipiter_gentilis_arrigonii"></span><i>A. g. arrigonii</i> (Kleinschmidt, 1903) – This is an island race found on the Mediterranean isles of <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">Sardinia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Corsica" title="Corsica">Corsica</a>. It averages smaller and weaker-footed than goshawks from the nominate race. The wing chord measures 293 to 308&#160;mm (11.5 to 12.1&#160;in) in males and 335 to 347&#160;mm (13.2 to 13.7&#160;in) in females. This race is typically a more blackish brown above with almost fully black head, while the underside is almost pure white and more heavily overlaid with black barring and conspicuous black shaft-streaks. This subspecies is not listed by all authorities but is often considered valid.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="anchor" id="Accipiter_gentilis_buteoides"></span><i>A. g. buteoides</i> (Menzbier, 1882) – This race is characteristic of the northern stretches of the western Eurasian range of goshawks, being found as a breeding species from northern <a href="/wiki/Fennoscandia" title="Fennoscandia">Fennoscandia</a> to western <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, ranging as far as the <a href="/wiki/Lena_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Lena River">Lena River</a>. In the eastern portion of its distribution, many birds may travel south to <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">central Asia</a> to winter. This is a large race, averaging larger than most populations of the nominate race but being about the same size as the big nominate goshawks with which they may overlap and interbreed with in Fennoscandia. The wing chord in males ranges from 308 to 345&#160;mm (12.1 to 13.6&#160;in) while that of females ranges from 340 to 388&#160;mm (13.4 to 15.3&#160;in). The body mass of males has been reported from 870 to 1,170&#160;g (1.92 to 2.58&#160;lb), with an average of 1,016&#160;g (2.240&#160;lb), while that of females is reportedly 1,190 to 1,850&#160;g (2.62 to 4.08&#160;lb), with an average of 1,355&#160;g (2.987&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Usually, this race is an altogether paler colour than the nominate, being blue-grey above with a dusky-grey crown and a broad supercilium. The underside is white with rather fine blackish-brown barring. Pale flecking on the feather shafts sometimes result in barred appearance on the contour feathers of the nape, back and upper wing. Many birds from this subspecies also have a tan to pale brown eye color. These two characteristics are sometimes considered typical of this race, but individuals are rather variable. In <a href="/wiki/Western_Siberia" title="Western Siberia">western Siberia</a>, about 10% of birds of this race are nearly pure white (similar to <i>albidus</i>) with varied indications of darker streaking.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="anchor" id="Accipiter_gentilis_albidus"></span><i>A. g. albidus</i> (Menzbier, 1882) – This race of goshawk is found in northeastern <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kamchatka" class="mw-redirect" title="Kamchatka">Kamchatka</a>. Many birds of this race travel south for the winter to <a href="/wiki/Transbaikal" title="Transbaikal">Transbaikalia</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ussuri_krai" title="Ussuri krai">Ussuriland</a>. This race continues the trend for goshawks to grow mildly larger eastbound in Eurasia and may be the largest known race based on the midpoint of known measurements of this race, although limited sample sizes of measured goshawks shows they broadly overlap in size with <i>A. g. buteoides</i> and large-bodied populations of <i>A. g. gentilis</i>. The wing chord can range from 316 to 346&#160;mm (12.4 to 13.6&#160;in) in males and from 370 to 388&#160;mm (14.6 to 15.3&#160;in) in females. Known males have scaled from 894 to 1,200&#160;g (1.971 to 2.646&#160;lb) while a small sample of females weighed have had a body mass between 1,300 and 1,750&#160;g (2.87 and 3.86&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> This is easily the palest race of northern goshawk. Many birds are pale grey above with much white about the head and very sparse barring below. However, about half of the goshawks of this race are more or less pure white, with at most only a few remnants of pale caramel flecking about the back or faint brownish markings elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gladkov_42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gladkov-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fischer_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="anchor" id="Accipiter_gentilis_schvedowi"></span><i>A. g. schvedowi</i> (Menzbier, 1882) – This race ranges from the <a href="/wiki/Ural_mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Ural mountains">Urals</a> east to the <a href="/wiki/Amur_Oblast" title="Amur Oblast">Amurland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ussuri_krai" title="Ussuri krai">Ussuriland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_China" title="Central China">west-central China</a> and sporadically as a breeder into <a href="/wiki/Sakhalin" title="Sakhalin">Sakhalin</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kuril_islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Kuril islands">Kuril islands</a>. <i>A. g. schvedowi</i> averages smaller than the other races on the mainland of Eurasia, with seemingly the highest sexual dimorphism of any goshawk race, possibly as an adaptation to prey partitioning in the exceptionally sparse wooded fringes of the desert-like <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppe</a> habitat that characterizes this race's range. The wing chord has been found to measure 298 to 323&#160;mm (11.7 to 12.7&#160;in) in males and 330 to 362&#160;mm (13.0 to 14.3&#160;in) in females.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Body mass of 15 males was found to be merely 357 to 600&#160;g (0.787 to 1.323&#160;lb) with a mean of 501&#160;g (1.105&#160;lb), the lowest adult weights known for this species, while two adult females scaled 1,000 and 1,170&#160;g (2.20 and 2.58&#160;lb), respectively, or more than twice as much on average.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Beyond its smaller size, its wings are reportedly relatively shorter and feet relatively smaller and weaker than other Eurasian races.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> In color, this race is typically a slate-grey above with a blackish head and is densely marked below with thin brown barring.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gladkov_42-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gladkov-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="anchor" id="Accipiter_gentilis_fujiyamae"></span><i>A. g. fujiyamae</i> (Swann &amp; Hartert, 1923) – Found through the species' range in Japan, from the islands of <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a> south to the large island of <a href="/wiki/Honshu" title="Honshu">Honshu</a>, in the latter down to as far south as forests a bit north of <a href="/wiki/Hiroshima" title="Hiroshima">Hiroshima</a>. A fairly small subspecies, it may average slightly smaller than <i>A. g. schvedowi</i> linearly, but it is less sexually dimorphic in size and weighs slightly more on average. The wing chord is the smallest known from any race, 286 to 300&#160;mm (11.3 to 11.8&#160;in) in males and 302 to 350&#160;mm (11.9 to 13.8&#160;in) in females.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> However, the weights of 22 males ranged from 602 to 848&#160;g (1.327 to 1.870&#160;lb), averaging 715&#160;g (1.576&#160;lb) while 22 females ranged from 929 to 1,265&#160;g (2.048 to 2.789&#160;lb), averaging 1,098&#160;g (2.421&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Shigeta_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shigeta-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The coloration of this race is not dissimilar from <i>A. g. schvedowi</i>, but is still darker slate above and they tend to have heavier barring below, probably being the darkest race on average, rivaled only by the similar insular race from the opposite side of the Pacific, <i>A. g. laingi</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gladkov_42-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gladkov-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shigeta_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shigeta-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="anchor" id="Accipiter_gentilis_atricapillus"></span><i>A. g. atricapillus</i> (<a href="/wiki/Alexander_Wilson_(ornithologist)" title="Alexander Wilson (ornithologist)">Wilson</a>, 1812) – Sometimes simply referred to as the <b>American goshawk</b>. This subspecies occupies a majority of the species' range in North America, excluding some islands of the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Northwest" title="Pacific Northwest">Pacific Northwest</a> and the southern part of the <a href="/wiki/American_Southwest" class="mw-redirect" title="American Southwest">American Southwest</a>. American goshawks are generally slightly smaller on average than most Eurasian ones, although there are regional differences in size that confirm mildly to <a href="/wiki/Bergmann%27s_rule" title="Bergmann&#39;s rule">Bergmann's rule</a> within this race. Furthermore, sexual dimorphism in size is notably less pronounced in American goshawks than in most Eurasian races. Overall, the wing chord is 308 to 337&#160;mm (12.1 to 13.3&#160;in) in males and 324 to 359&#160;mm (12.8 to 14.1&#160;in) in females.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Size within <i>atricapillus</i> based on body mass seems to be highest in interior <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, followed by the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a>, is intermediate in the northwest United States from eastern <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Dakotas" class="mw-redirect" title="Dakotas">Dakotas</a> as well as in southeast Alaska thence decreasing mildly along the Pacific in <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a> and California and smallest of all within the race in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Basin" title="Great Basin">Great Basin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Plateau" title="Colorado Plateau">Colorado Plateau</a> states (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a> and northern and central <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>). Conspicuously, wing size did not correspond to variations in body mass and more southerly goshawks were frequently longer winged than the more massive northerly ones.<sup id="cite_ref-Palmer_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palmer-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smith_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Male <i>atricapillus</i> goshawk have been found to weigh from 655 to 1,200&#160;g (1.444 to 2.646&#160;lb) and females from 758 to 1,562&#160;g (1.671 to 3.444&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Palmer_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palmer-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The lightest reported mean weights were from goshawks in northern and central Arizona, weighing a mean of 680&#160;g (1.50&#160;lb) in males and 935&#160;g (2.061&#160;lb) while the highest were from a small sample of Alaskan goshawks which weighed some 905&#160;g (1.995&#160;lb) in males and 1,190&#160;g (2.62&#160;lb) in females.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Almost identical mean weights for goshawks as in <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> were recorded for goshawks from <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a> as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Palmer_41-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palmer-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> This race is typically a blue-gray color above with a boldly contrasting black head and broad white supercilia. American goshawks are often grayish below with fine gray waving barring and, compared to most Eurasian goshawks, rather apparent black shaft streaks which in combination create a vermiculated effect that is all-together messier looking than in most Eurasian birds. From a distance, <i>atricapillus</i> can easily appear solidly all-gray from the front.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Due to this, the adult goshawk in America is sometimes called the “gray ghost”, a name also somewhat more commonly used for adult male <a href="/wiki/Hen_harrier" title="Hen harrier">hen harriers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rashid_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rashid-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>  Birds from mainland Alaska tend to be paler overall with more pale flecking than other American goshawks.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="anchor" id="Accipiter_gentilis_laingi"></span><i>A. g. laingi</i> (Tavernier, 1940) – This insular race is found on the <a href="/wiki/Queen_Charlotte_Island" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen Charlotte Island">Queen Charlotte Islands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vancouver_Island" title="Vancouver Island">Vancouver Island</a>. This subspecies is slightly smaller than the goshawks found on the mainland and is linearly the smallest race on average in North America. The wing chord of males can range from 312 to 325&#160;mm (12.3 to 12.8&#160;in) and that of females is 332 to 360&#160;mm (13.1 to 14.2&#160;in) and is on average nearly 5% smaller than those sampled goshawks from the nearby mainland.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnson1989_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson1989-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> These goshawks are characteristically darker than mainland goshawks with the black of the crown extending to the interscapulars. The underside is a sootier gray overall.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnson1989_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson1989-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="anchor" id="Accipiter_gentilis_apache"></span><i>A. g. apache</i> (van Rossem, 1938) – The range of this subspecies extends from southern Arizona and <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a> down throughout the species' range in <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>. This subspecies has the longest median wing size of any race, running contrary to Bergmann's rule that northern birds should outsize southern ones in widely distributed temperate species. In males the <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">wing chord</a> ranges from 344 to 354&#160;mm (13.5 to 13.9&#160;in) while in females it ranges from 365 to 390&#160;mm (14.4 to 15.4&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> However, in terms of body mass, it is only slightly heavier than the goshawks found discontinuously somewhat to the north in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Basin" title="Great Basin">Great Basin</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Plateau" title="Colorado Plateau">Colorado Plateau</a> and lighter than the heaviest known American goshawks from Alaska, Alberta and <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a> despite exceeding the goshawks from these areas in wing size. The weight of 49 males ranged from 631 to 744&#160;g (1.391 to 1.640&#160;lb), averaging 704&#160;g (1.552&#160;lb), while that of 88 females from two studies ranged from 845 to 1,265&#160;g (1.863 to 2.789&#160;lb), averaging 1,006&#160;g (2.218&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Aside from its overall larger size, <i>apache</i> reportedly averages larger in foot size than most other American goshawks. Birds of this race tend to be darker than other American goshawks aside from the <i>laingi</i> type birds. Due to its shortage of distinct features beyond proportions, this is considered one of the more weakly separated among current separate subspecies, with some authors considering it merely a clinal variation of <i>atricapillus</i>. Even the greater wing size in southern birds follows a trend for the wing chord to increase in size in the south on the contrary to body mass.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smith_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Similar_species">Similar species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Similar species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AccipterGentilisJuvenileFlight1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/AccipterGentilisJuvenileFlight1.jpg/220px-AccipterGentilisJuvenileFlight1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/AccipterGentilisJuvenileFlight1.jpg/330px-AccipterGentilisJuvenileFlight1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/AccipterGentilisJuvenileFlight1.jpg/440px-AccipterGentilisJuvenileFlight1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2010" data-file-height="1340" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AccipterGentilisJuvenileFlight1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Juvenile in flight, the most likely age and condition to mistake a goshawk for another species</div></div></div>
<p>The juvenile plumage of the species may cause some confusion, especially with other <i>Accipiter</i> juveniles. Unlike other northern <i>Accipiters</i>, the adult northern goshawk never has a rusty color to its underside barring.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>  In <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a>, the smaller male goshawk is sometimes confused with a female <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_sparrowhawk" title="Eurasian sparrowhawk">sparrowhawk</a>, but is still notably larger, much bulkier and has relatively longer wings, which are more pointed and less boxy. Sparrowhawks tend to fly in a frequently flapping, fluttering type flight. Wing beats of northern goshawks are deeper, more deliberate, and on average slower than those of the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_sparrowhawk" title="Eurasian sparrowhawk">Eurasian sparrowhawk</a> or the two other North American <i>Accipiters</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The classic <i>Accipiter</i> flight is a characteristic "flap flap, glide", but the goshawk, with its greater wing area, can sometimes be seen steadily soaring in migration (smaller <i>Accipiters</i> almost always need to flap to stay aloft).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hangingrocktower_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hangingrocktower-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> juveniles are sometimes confused with the smaller <a href="/wiki/Cooper%27s_hawk" title="Cooper&#39;s hawk">Cooper's hawk</a> (<i>Accipiter cooperii</i>), especially between small male goshawks and large female Cooper's hawks. Unlike in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> with sparrowhawks, Cooper's hawks can have a largish appearance and juveniles may be regularly mistaken for the usually less locally numerous goshawk. However, the juvenile goshawk displays a heavier, vertical streaking pattern on chest and abdomen, with the juvenile Cooper's hawk streaking frequently (but not always) in a “teardrop” pattern wherein the streaking appears to taper at the top, as opposed to the more even streaking of the goshawk. The goshawk sometimes seems to have a shorter tail relative to its much broader body. Although there appears to be a size overlap between small male goshawks and large female Cooper's hawks, <a href="/wiki/Morphometric" class="mw-redirect" title="Morphometric">morphometric</a> measurements (wing and tail length) of both species demonstrate no such overlap, although weight overlap can rarely occur due to variation in seasonal condition and food intake at time of weighing.<sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Rarely, in the southern stretches of its Asian wintering range, the northern goshawk may live alongside the <a href="/wiki/Crested_goshawk" title="Crested goshawk">crested goshawk</a> (<i>Accipiter trivirgatus</i>) which is smaller (roughly Cooper's hawk-sized) and has a slight crest as well as a distinct mixture of denser streaks and bars below and no supercilia.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coopers-Hawk-on-a-Fence.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Coopers-Hawk-on-a-Fence.jpg/170px-Coopers-Hawk-on-a-Fence.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="238" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Coopers-Hawk-on-a-Fence.jpg/255px-Coopers-Hawk-on-a-Fence.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Coopers-Hawk-on-a-Fence.jpg/340px-Coopers-Hawk-on-a-Fence.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1394" data-file-height="1951" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coopers-Hawk-on-a-Fence.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Large juvenile <a href="/wiki/Cooper%27s_hawk" title="Cooper&#39;s hawk">Cooper's hawks</a> such as this are at times mistaken for a goshawk</div></div></div>
<p>Northern goshawks are sometimes mistaken for species even outside of the genus <i>Accipiter</i> especially as juveniles of each respective species. In <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, four species of <a href="/wiki/Buteoninae" title="Buteoninae">buteonine hawk</a> (all four of which are smaller than goshawks to a certain degree) may be confused with them on occasion despite the differing proportions of these hawks, which all have longer wings and shorter tails relative to their size. A species so similar it is sometimes nicknamed the “Mexican goshawk”, <a href="/wiki/Gray_hawk" title="Gray hawk">gray hawk</a> (<i>Buteo plagiatus</i>) juveniles (overlapping with true goshawks in the southwest United States into Mexico) have contrasting face pattern with bold dusky eye-stripes, dark eyes, barred thighs and a bold white “U” on the uppertail coverts. The <a href="/wiki/Roadside_hawk" title="Roadside hawk">roadside hawk</a> (<i>Rupornis magnirostris</i>) (rarely in same range in Mexico) is noticeably smaller with paddle-shaped wings, barred lower breast and a buff “U” on undertail coverts in young birds. Somewhat less likely to confuse despite their broader extent of overlap are the <a href="/wiki/Red-shouldered_hawk" title="Red-shouldered hawk">red-shouldered hawk</a> (<i>Buteo lineatus</i>) which have a narrow white-barred, dark-looking tail, bold white crescents on their primaries and dark wing edges and the <a href="/wiki/Broad-winged_hawk" title="Broad-winged hawk">broad-winged hawk</a> (<i>Buteo playpterus</i>) which also has dark wing edges and a differing tapered wing shape. Even wintering <a href="/wiki/Gyrfalcon" title="Gyrfalcon">gyrfalcon</a> (<i>Falco rusticolus</i>) juveniles have been mistaken for goshawks and vice versa on occasion, especially when observed distantly perched. However, the bulkier, broader headed yet relatively shorter tailed falcon still has many tell-tale falcon characteristics like pointed, longer wings, a brown malar stripe as well as its more extensive barring both above and below.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behavior">Behavior</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Behavior">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Territoriality">Territoriality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Territoriality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Goshawk_in_flight.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Goshawk_in_flight.JPG/220px-Goshawk_in_flight.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Goshawk_in_flight.JPG/330px-Goshawk_in_flight.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Goshawk_in_flight.JPG/440px-Goshawk_in_flight.JPG 2x" data-file-width="677" data-file-height="451" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Goshawk_in_flight.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Adult goshawks maintain territories with display flights</div></div></div>
<p>The northern goshawk is always found solitarily or in pairs. This species is highly territorial as are most raptorial birds, maintaining regularly spaced home ranges that constitute their territory. Territories are maintained by adults in display flights. During nesting, the home ranges of goshawk pairs are from 600 to 4,000&#160;ha (1,500 to 9,900 acres) and these vicinities tend to be vigorously defended both to maintain rights to their nests and mates as well as the ranges’ prey base.<sup id="cite_ref-Squires_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Squires-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> During display flight goshawks may engage in single or mutual high-circling.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Each sex tends to defend the territory from others of their own sex.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Territorial flights may occur almost through the year, but peak from January to April. Such flights may include slow-flapping with exaggerated high deep beats interspersed with long glides and undulations.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In general, territorial fights are resolved without physical contact, often with one (usually a younger bird seeking a territory) retreats while the other approaches in a <a href="/wiki/Harrier_(bird)" title="Harrier (bird)">harrier</a>-like warning flight, flashing its white underside at the intruder. If the incoming goshawk does not leave the vicinity, the defending goshawk may increase the exaggerated quality of its flight including a mildly undulating wave-formed rowing flight and the rowing flight with its neck held in a <a href="/wiki/Heron" title="Heron">heron</a>-like S to elevate the head and maximally expose the pale breast as a territorial threat display. Territorial skirmishes may on occasion escalate to physical fights in which mortalities may occur. In actual fights, goshawks fall grappling to the ground as they attempt to strike each other with talons.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Link_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Link-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schnurre_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schnurre-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Migration">Migration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Migration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although at times considered rather sedentary for a northern raptor species, the northern goshawk is a <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">partial migrant</a>. Migratory movements generally occur between September and November (occasionally extending throughout December) in the fall and February to April in the spring. Spring migration is less extensive and more poorly known than fall migration, but seems to peak late March to early April. Some birds up to as far north as northern <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> and central <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> may remain on territory throughout the winter.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Squires_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Squires-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Northern goshawks from northern <a href="/wiki/Fennoscandia" title="Fennoscandia">Fennoscandia</a> have been recorded traveling up to 1,640&#160;km (1,020&#160;mi) away from first banding but adults seldom are recorded more than 300&#160;km (190&#160;mi) from their summer range. In Sweden, young birds distributed an average of 377&#160;km (234&#160;mi) in the north to an average of 70&#160;km (43&#160;mi) in the south.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In northern Sweden, young generally disperse somewhat south, whereas in south and central Sweden, they typically distributed to the south (but not usually across the 5-km <a href="/wiki/Kattegat" title="Kattegat">Kattegat straits</a>). On the other hand, 4.3% of the southern Swedish goshawks actually moved north.
