Migrating goshawks seem to avoid crossing water, but sparrowhawks seems to be able to do so more regularly.<sup id="cite_ref-Marcström_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marcström-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hoglund_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hoglund-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">central Europe</a>, few birds travel more than 30&#160;km (19&#160;mi) throughout the year, a few juveniles have exceptionally been recorded traveling up to 300&#160;km (190&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In Eurasia, very small numbers of migratory northern goshawks cross the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Gibraltar" title="Strait of Gibraltar">Strait of Gibraltar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bosporus" title="Bosporus">Bosporus</a> in autumn but further east more significant winter range expansions may extend from northern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> &amp; southern <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenia</a> to <a href="/wiki/Lake_Aral" class="mw-redirect" title="Lake Aral">Aral</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Lake_Balkhash" title="Lake Balkhash">Balkhash lakes</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmir</a> to <a href="/wiki/Assam" title="Assam">Assam</a>, extreme northwestern <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southern_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Southern China">southern China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Islands" title="Ryukyu Islands">Ryukyu Islands</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>. Migratory goshawks in North America may move down to <a href="/wiki/Baja_California" title="Baja California">Baja California</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sinaloa" title="Sinaloa">Sinaloa</a> and into most of <a href="/wiki/West_Texas" title="West Texas">west Texas</a>, but generally in non-irruptive years, goshawks winter no further south than <a href="/wiki/Nebraska" title="Nebraska">Nebraska</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a>, eastern <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a> and western <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Squires_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Squires-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Some periodic eruptions to nearly as far as the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a> have been recorded at no fewer than 10 years apart. In one case, a female that was banded in <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a> was recovered 1,860&#160;km (1,160&#160;mi) in <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, a first ever record of the species in that state.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Prey availability may primarily dictate the proportion of goshawk populations that migrate and the selection of wintering areas, followed by the presence of snow which may aid prey capture in the short-term but in the long-term is likely to cause higher goshawk mortality.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marcström_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marcström-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Doyle_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doyle-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Showing the high variability of migratory movements, in one study of winter movements of adult female goshawks that bred in high-elevation forests of <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a>, about 36% migrated 100 to 613&#160;km (62 to 381&#160;mi) to the general south, 22% migrated farther than that distance, 8.3% migrated less far, 2.7% went north instead of south and 31% stayed throughout winter on their breeding territory.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Irruptive movements seem to occur for northern populations, i.e. those of the <a href="/wiki/Taiga" title="Taiga">boreal forests</a> in <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>, and possibly <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, with more equal sex ratio of movement and a strong southward tendency of movements in years where prey such as <a href="/wiki/Hare" title="Hare">hares</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grouse" title="Grouse">grouse</a> crash.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Male young goshawks tend to disperse farther than females, which is unusual in birds, including raptors.<sup id="cite_ref-Marcström_68-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marcström-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> It has been speculated that larger female juveniles displace male juveniles, forcing them to disperse farther, to the incidental benefit of the species’ genetic diversity. In <a href="/wiki/Cedar_Grove,_Wisconsin" title="Cedar Grove, Wisconsin">Cedar Grove</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>, there were more than twice as many juvenile males than females recorded migrating.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> At the hawk watch at <a href="/wiki/Cape_May_Point_State_Park" title="Cape May Point State Park">Cape May Point State Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>, few adult males and no adult females have been recorded in fall migration apart from irruptive years, indicating that migration is more important to juveniles.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>  More juveniles were recorded migrating than adults in several years of study from Sweden.<sup id="cite_ref-Marcström_68-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marcström-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> In northern <i>Accipiters</i> including the goshawk, there seems to be multiple peaks in numbers of migrants, an observation that suggests partial segregation by age and sex.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dietary_biology">Dietary biology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Dietary biology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hunting_behavior">Hunting behavior</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Hunting behavior">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_gentilis_Viladecans.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Accipiter_gentilis_Viladecans.jpg/220px-Accipiter_gentilis_Viladecans.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Accipiter_gentilis_Viladecans.jpg/330px-Accipiter_gentilis_Viladecans.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Accipiter_gentilis_Viladecans.jpg/440px-Accipiter_gentilis_Viladecans.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1451" data-file-height="1004" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_gentilis_Viladecans.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Goshawks are particularly agile hunters of the woodlands</div></div></div>
<p>As typical of the genus <i>Accipiter</i> (as well as unrelated forest-dwelling raptors of various lineages), the northern goshawk has relatively short wings and a long tail which make it ideally adapted to engaging in brief but agile and twisting hunting flights through dense vegetation of wooded environments.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> This species is a powerful hunter, taking birds and mammals in a variety of woodland habitats, often utilizing a combination of speed and obstructing cover to ambush their victims. Goshawks often forage in adjoining habitat types, such as the edge of a forest and meadow. Hunting habitat can be variable, as in a comparison of habitats used in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> found that only 8% of landscapes used were woodlands whereas in <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> 73-76% of the habitat used was woodland, albeit normally within 200&#160;m (660&#160;ft) of an opening.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, goshawks are generally rather more likely than those from Eurasia to hunt within the confines of mature forest, excluding areas where prey numbers are larger outside of the forest, such as where scrub-dwelling <a href="/wiki/Cottontail" class="mw-redirect" title="Cottontail">cottontails</a> are profuse.<sup id="cite_ref-Bright-Smith_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bright-Smith-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> One study from central <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> found that locally goshawks typically hunt within the largest patches of mature forests, selecting second growth forest less than half as often as its prevalence in the local environment.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The northern goshawk is typically considered a perch-hunter. Hunting efforts are punctuated by a series of quick flights low to the ground, interspersed with brief periods of scanning for unsuspecting prey from elevated perches (short duration sit-and-wait predatory movements). These flights are meant to be inconspicuous, averaging about 83 seconds in males and 94 seconds in females, and prey pursuits may be abandoned if the victims become aware of the goshawk too quickly.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> More sporadically, northern goshawks may watch from prey from a high soar or gliding flight above the canopy.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> One study in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> found an exceptional 80% of hunting efforts to be done from a high soar but the author admitted that he was probably biased by the conspicuousness of this method.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> In comparison, a study from <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> found that 95% of hunting efforts were from perches.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> A strong bias for <a href="/wiki/Pigeon" class="mw-redirect" title="Pigeon">pigeons</a> as prey and a largely urbanized environment in Germany explains the local prevalence of hunting from a soaring flight, as the urban environment provides ample thermals and obstructing tall buildings which are ideal for hunting pigeons on the wing.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Northern goshawks rarely vary from their perch-hunting style that typifies the initial part of their hunt but seems to be able to show nearly endless variation to the concluding pursuit.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Hunting goshawks seem to not only utilize thick vegetation to block them from view for their prey (as typical of <i>Accipiters</i>) but, while hunting flying birds, they seem to be able to adjust their flight level so the prey is unable to see its hunter past their own tails.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Once a prey item is selected, a short tail-chase may occur. The northern goshawk is capable of considerable, sustained, horizontal speed in pursuit of prey with speeds of 38&#160;mph (61&#160;km/h) reported.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> While pursuing prey, northern goshawks has been described both “reckless” and “fearless”, able to pursue their prey through nearly any conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> There are various times goshawks have been observed going on foot to pursue prey, at times running without hesitation (in a <a href="/wiki/Crow" title="Crow">crow</a>-like, but more hurried gait) into dense thickets and brambles (especially in pursuit of <a href="/wiki/Galliforms" class="mw-redirect" title="Galliforms">galliforms</a> trying to escape), as well as into water (i.e. usually <a href="/wiki/Waterfowl" class="mw-redirect" title="Waterfowl">waterfowl</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Schnell_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schnell-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Anecdotal cases have been reported when goshawks have pursue domestic prey into barns and even houses.<sup id="cite_ref-Rashid_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rashid-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Prey pursuits may become rather prolonged depending upon the goshawk's determination and hunger, ranging up to 15 minutes while harrying a terrified, agile <a href="/wiki/Squirrel" title="Squirrel">squirrel</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hare" title="Hare">hare</a>, and occasional pair hunting may benefit goshawks going after agile prey. As is recorded in many accipitrids, hunting in pairs (or “<a href="/wiki/Pack_hunter" title="Pack hunter">tandem hunting</a>”) normally consist of a breeding pair, with one bird flying conspicuously to distract the prey, while the other swoops in from behind to ambush the victim.<sup id="cite_ref-Fischer_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> When gliding down from a perch to capture prey, a goshawk may not even beat its wings, rendering its flight nearly silent.<sup id="cite_ref-Beebe_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beebe-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Prey is killed by driving the talons into the quarry and squeezing while the head is held back to avoid flailing limbs, frequently followed by a kneading action until the prey stops struggling. Kills are normally consumed on the ground by juvenile or non-breeding goshawks (more rarely an elevated perch or old nest) or taken to a low perch by breeding goshawks. Habitual perches are used for dismantling prey especially in the breeding season, often called “plucking perches”, which may be fallen logs, bent-over trees, stumps or rocks and can see years of usage.<sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Northern goshawks often leave larger portions of their prey uneaten than other raptors, with limbs, many feathers and fur and other body parts strewn near kill sites and plucking perches, and are helpful to distinguish their kills from other raptors such as large owls, who usually eat everything.<sup id="cite_ref-Rashid_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rashid-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> The daily food requirements of a single goshawks are around 120 to 150&#160;g (4.2 to 5.3&#160;oz) and most kills can feed a goshawk for 1 to 3 days.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Northern goshawks sometimes cache prey on tree branches or wedged in a crotch between branches for up to 32 hours. This is done primarily during the nestling stage.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Hunting success rates have been very roughly estimated at 15–30%, within average range for a bird of prey, but may be reported as higher elsewhere. One study claimed hunting success rates for pursuing <a href="/wiki/Rabbit" title="Rabbit">rabbits</a> was 60% and <a href="/wiki/Corvid" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvid">corvids</a> was 63.8%.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prey_spectrum">Prey spectrum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Prey spectrum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Northern_Goshawk_-_Catalonia_-_Spain_S4E8845_(25217894495).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Northern_Goshawk_-_Catalonia_-_Spain_S4E8845_%2825217894495%29.jpg/220px-Northern_Goshawk_-_Catalonia_-_Spain_S4E8845_%2825217894495%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Northern_Goshawk_-_Catalonia_-_Spain_S4E8845_%2825217894495%29.jpg/330px-Northern_Goshawk_-_Catalonia_-_Spain_S4E8845_%2825217894495%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Northern_Goshawk_-_Catalonia_-_Spain_S4E8845_%2825217894495%29.jpg/440px-Northern_Goshawk_-_Catalonia_-_Spain_S4E8845_%2825217894495%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1140" data-file-height="760" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Northern_Goshawk_-_Catalonia_-_Spain_S4E8845_(25217894495).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Northern goshawks most often prey on birds, especially in <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a></div></div></div>
<p>Northern goshawks are usually opportunistic predators, as are most <a href="/wiki/Bird_of_prey" title="Bird of prey">birds of prey</a>. The most important prey species are small to medium-sized <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> and medium to large-sized birds found in forest, edge and scrub habitats.<sup id="cite_ref-Reynolds_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Primary prey selection varies considerably not just at the regional but also the individual level as the primary food species can be dramatically different in nests just a few kilometers apart.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> As is typical in various birds of prey, small prey tends to be underrepresented in prey remains below habitual perches and nests (as only present in skeletal remains within pellets) whereas pellets underrepresent large prey (which is usually dismantled away from the nest) and so a combined study of both remains and pellets is recommended to get a full picture of goshawks’ diets.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rutz_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rutz-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Prey selection also varies by season and a majority of dietary studies are conducted within the breeding season, leaving a possibility of bias for male-selected prey, whereas recent advanced in radio-tagging have allowed a broader picture of goshawk's fairly different winter diet (without needing to kill goshawks to examine their stomach contents).<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rutz_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rutz-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>  Northern goshawks have a varied diet that has reportedly included over 500 species from across its range, and at times their prey spectrum can extend to nearly any available kind of bird or mammal except the particularly large varieties as well as atypical prey including <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> However, a few prey families dominate the diet in most parts of the range, namely <a href="/wiki/Corvid" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvid">corvids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pigeon" class="mw-redirect" title="Pigeon">pigeons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grouse" title="Grouse">grouse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phasianidae" title="Phasianidae">pheasants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thrush_(bird)" title="Thrush (bird)">thrushes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Woodpecker" title="Woodpecker">woodpeckers</a> (in roughly descending order of importance) among birds and <a href="/wiki/Squirrel" title="Squirrel">squirrels</a> (mainly <a href="/wiki/Tree_squirrel" title="Tree squirrel">tree squirrels</a> but also <a href="/wiki/Ground_squirrel" title="Ground squirrel">ground squirrels</a> especially in North America) and <a href="/wiki/Leporidae" title="Leporidae">rabbits and hares</a> among mammals.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smithers2005_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smithers2005-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-animaldiversity_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-animaldiversity-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lewis2006_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis2006-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Birds are usually the primary prey in Europe, constituting 76.5% of the diet in 17 studies. In North America, by comparison, they constitute 47.8% in 33 studies and mammals account for a nearly equal portion of the diet and in some areas rather dominate the food spectrum.<sup id="cite_ref-Kennedy_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kennedy-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bosakowski_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bosakowski-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Studies have shown that from several parts of the Eurasian continent from <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Ural_mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Ural mountains">Ural mountains</a> mammals contributed only about 9% of the breeding season diet. However, mammals may be slightly underrepresented in Eurasian data because of the little-studied presence of mammals as a food source in winter, particularly in the western and southern portions of Europe where the lack of snowfall can allow large numbers of <a href="/wiki/Rabbit" title="Rabbit">rabbits</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Staple prey for northern goshawks usually weighs between 50 and 2,000&#160;g (1.8 and 70.5&#160;oz), with average prey weights per individual studies typically between 215 and 770&#160;g (7.6 and 27.2&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hoglund_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hoglund-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reynolds_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zawadzka_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zawadzka-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> There is some difference in size and type between the prey caught by males and larger females. Prey selection between sexes is more disparate in the more highly dimorphic races from Eurasia than those from North America. In the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, male prey averaged 277&#160;g (9.8&#160;oz) whereas female prey averaged 505&#160;g (17.8&#160;oz), thus a rough 45% difference .<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In comparison, the average prey caught by each sex in <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a> was 281.5&#160;g (9.93&#160;oz) and 380.4&#160;g (13.42&#160;oz), respectively, or around a 26% difference.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Northern goshawks often select young prey during spring and summer, attacking both nestling and fledgling birds and infant and yearling mammals, as such prey is often easiest to catch and convenient to bring to the nest. In general, goshawks in <a href="/wiki/Fennoscandia" title="Fennoscandia">Fennoscandia</a>, shift their prey selection to when the birds produce their young: first <a href="/wiki/Waterfowl" class="mw-redirect" title="Waterfowl">waterfowl</a>, then quickly to <a href="/wiki/Corvid" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvid">corvids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thrush_(bird)" title="Thrush (bird)">thrushes</a> and then lastly to <a href="/wiki/Grouse" title="Grouse">grouse</a>, even though adults are also freely caught opportunistically for all these prey types.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-profitability_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-profitability-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> This is fairly different from <a href="/wiki/Vendsyssel" title="Vendsyssel">Vendsyssel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, where mostly adult birds were caught except for thrushes and corvids, as in these two groups, the goshawks caught mostly fledglings.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Corvids">Corvids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Corvids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Overall, one prey family that is known to be taken in nearly every part of the goshawk's range is the corvids, although they do not necessarily dominate the diet in all areas. Some 24 species have been reported in the diet. The second most commonly reported prey species in breeding season dietary studies from both Europe and North America are both large <a href="/wiki/Jay" title="Jay">jays</a>, the 160&#160;g (5.6&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_jay" title="Eurasian jay">Eurasian jay</a> (<i>Glarius glandarius</i>) and the 128&#160;g (4.5&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Steller%27s_jay" title="Steller&#39;s jay">Steller's jay</a> (<i>Cyanocitta stelleri</i>). These species were recorded in studies from northeastern <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Apennine_Mountains" title="Apennine Mountains">Apennines</a> of <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> (where the Eurasian jays made up a quarter of the food by number) and in northwestern <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kaibab_Plateau" title="Kaibab Plateau">Kaibab Plateau</a> of <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a> (where the Steller's made up 37% by number) as the main prey species by number. The conspicuously loud vocalizations, somewhat sluggish flight (when hunting adult or post-fledging individuals) and moderate size of these jays make them ideal for prey-gathering male goshawks.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reynolds_96-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zawadzka_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zawadzka-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Penteriani_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penteriani-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Boal_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boal-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Another medium-sized corvid, the 218&#160;g (7.7&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_magpie" title="Eurasian magpie">Eurasian magpie</a> (<i>Pica pica</i>) is also amongst the most widely reported secondary prey species for goshawks there. Magpies, like large jays, are rather slow fliers and can be handily outpaced by a pursuing goshawk.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rashid_54-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rashid-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rutz2_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rutz2-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Verdal_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Verdal-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Some authors claim that taking of large corvids is a rare behavior, due of their intelligence and complex sociality which in turn impart formidable group defenses and mobbing capabilities. One estimation claimed this to be done by about 1–2% of adult goshawks during the breeding season (based largely on studies from Sweden and England), however, on the contrary many goshawks do routinely hunt crows and similar species. In fact, there are some recorded cases where goshawks were able to exploit such mobbing behavior in order to trick crows into close range, where the mob victim suddenly turned to grab one predaceously.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> In the following areas <i><a href="/wiki/Corvus" title="Corvus">Corvus</a></i> species were the leading prey by number: the 440&#160;g (16&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Hooded_crow" title="Hooded crow">hooded crow</a> (<i>Corvus cornix</i>) in the <a href="/wiki/Ural_mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Ural mountains">Ural mountains</a> (9% by number), the 245&#160;g (8.6&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Western_jackdaw" title="Western jackdaw">western jackdaw</a> (<i>Corvus monedula</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Sierra_de_Guadarrama" title="Sierra de Guadarrama">Sierra de Guadarrama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> (36.4% by number), the 453&#160;g (0.999&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Rook_(bird)" title="Rook (bird)">rook</a> (<i>Corvus frugilegus</i>) in the <a href="/wiki/Zhambyl_District,_North_Kazakhstan_Region" title="Zhambyl District, North Kazakhstan Region">Zhambyl district</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a> (36.6% by number) and the 457&#160;g (1.008&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/American_crow" title="American crow">American crow</a> (<i>Corvus brachyrhynchos</i>) in <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a> (44.8% by number).<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Araujo_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Araujo-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Meng_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meng-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Despite evidence that northern goshawks avoid nesting near <a href="/wiki/Common_raven" title="Common raven">common ravens</a> (<i>Corvus corax</i>), the largest widespread corvid (about the same size as a goshawk at 1,040&#160;g (2.29&#160;lb)) and a formidable opponent even one-on-one, they are even known to prey on ravens seldomly.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zawadzka_107-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zawadzka-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Corvids taken have ranged in size from the 72&#160;g (2.5&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Canada_jay" title="Canada jay">Canada jay</a> (<i>Perisoreus canadensis</i>) to the raven.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Watson_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pigeons_and_doves">Pigeons and doves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Pigeons and doves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_gentilis_by_Iosto_Doneddu.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Accipiter_gentilis_by_Iosto_Doneddu.jpg/220px-Accipiter_gentilis_by_Iosto_Doneddu.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Accipiter_gentilis_by_Iosto_Doneddu.jpg/330px-Accipiter_gentilis_by_Iosto_Doneddu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Accipiter_gentilis_by_Iosto_Doneddu.jpg/440px-Accipiter_gentilis_by_Iosto_Doneddu.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_gentilis_by_Iosto_Doneddu.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Adult on <a href="/wiki/Corsica" title="Corsica">Corsica</a> with its fresh prey, a <a href="/wiki/Common_wood_pigeon" title="Common wood pigeon">common wood pigeon</a></div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, the leading prey species numerically (the main prey species in 41% of 32 European studies largely focused on the nesting season) is the 352&#160;g (12.4&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Rock_pigeon" class="mw-redirect" title="Rock pigeon">rock pigeon</a> (<i>Columba livia</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Although the predominance of rock pigeons in urban environments that host goshawks such as the <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">German</a> cities of <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a> (where they constituted 36% by number and nearly 45% by weight of the local diet) or <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a> is predictable, evidence shows that these development-clinging pigeons are sought out even within ample conserved woodland from <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> to <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rutz2_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rutz2-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Skilsky_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Skilsky-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Abuladze_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abuladze-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> In areas where goshawk restrict their hunting forays to field and forest, they often catch another numerous pigeon, the 490&#160;g (1.08&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Common_wood_pigeon" title="Common wood pigeon">common wood pigeon</a> (<i>Columba palumbus</i>) (the largest pigeon the goshawk naturally encounters and is known to hunt). The latter species was the main prey in the diet of northern goshawks from in the <a href="/wiki/Germany-Netherlands_border" class="mw-redirect" title="Germany-Netherlands border">Germany-Netherlands border</a> area (37.7% of 4125 prey items) and <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> (25.1% by number and 30.5% by biomass of total prey).<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Opdam_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Opdam-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> It has been theorized that male goshawks in peri-urban regions may be better suited with their higher agility to ambushing rock pigeons in and amongst various manmade structures whereas females may be better suited due to the higher overall speeds to taking out common wood-pigeons, as these typically forage in wood-cloaked but relatively open fields; however males are efficient predators of common wood-pigeons as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Studies have proven that, while hunting rock pigeons, goshawks quite often select the oddly colored pigeons out of flocks as prey, whether the plumage of the flock is predominantly dark or light hued, they disproportionately often select individuals of the other color. This preference is apparently more pronounced in older, experienced goshawks and there is some evidence that the males who select oddly-colored pigeons have higher average productivity during breeding.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Around eight additional species of <a href="/wiki/Pigeon" class="mw-redirect" title="Pigeon">pigeon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dove" class="mw-redirect" title="Dove">dove</a> have turned up in the goshawks diet from throughout the range but only in small numbers and in most of North America, goshawks take pigeons less commonly than in Eurasia.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bosakowski2_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bosakowski2-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> One exception is in <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a> where the <a href="/wiki/Mourning_dove" title="Mourning dove">mourning dove</a> (<i>Zenaida macroura</i>), the smallest known pigeon or dove the goshawk has hunted at 119&#160;g (4.2&#160;oz), was the second most numerous prey species.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Becker_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Becker-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gamebirds">Gamebirds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Gamebirds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hawk_and_Black-Game_(Bruno_Liljefors)_-_Nationalmuseum_-_19423.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Hawk_and_Black-Game_%28Bruno_Liljefors%29_-_Nationalmuseum_-_19423.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Hawk_and_Black-Game_%28Bruno_Liljefors%29_-_Nationalmuseum_-_19423.tif.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Hawk_and_Black-Game_%28Bruno_Liljefors%29_-_Nationalmuseum_-_19423.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Hawk_and_Black-Game_%28Bruno_Liljefors%29_-_Nationalmuseum_-_19423.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Hawk_and_Black-Game_%28Bruno_Liljefors%29_-_Nationalmuseum_-_19423.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Hawk_and_Black-Game_%28Bruno_Liljefors%29_-_Nationalmuseum_-_19423.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3496" data-file-height="2454" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hawk_and_Black-Game_(Bruno_Liljefors)_-_Nationalmuseum_-_19423.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Hawk and Black-Game</i> (<a href="/wiki/Bruno_Liljefors" title="Bruno Liljefors">Bruno Liljefors</a>, 1884), a painting of a goshawk at the moment of catching a <a href="/wiki/Black_grouse" title="Black grouse">black grouse</a></div></div></div>
<p>The northern goshawk is in some parts of its range considered a specialized predator of <a href="/wiki/Galliformes" title="Galliformes">gamebirds</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Grouse" title="Grouse">grouse</a>. All told 33 species of this order have turned up in their diet, including most of the species either native to or introduced in North America and Europe. Numerically, only in the well-studied <a href="/wiki/Taiga" title="Taiga">taiga</a> habitats of <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> and some areas of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_United_States" title="Eastern United States">eastern United States</a> do grouse typically take a dominant position. Elsewhere in the range, gamebirds are often secondary in number but often remain one of the most important contributors of prey biomass to nests. With their general ground-dwelling habits, gamebirds tend to be fairly easy for goshawks to overtake if they remain unseen and, if made aware of the goshawk, the prey chooses to run rather than fly. If frightened too soon, gamebirds may take flight and may be chased for some time, although the capture rates are reduced considerably when this occurs. Pre-fledgling chicks of gamebirds are particularly vulnerable due to the fact that they can only run when being pursued.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In several parts of <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>, forest grouse have historically been important prey for goshawks both in and out of the nesting season, principally the 1,080&#160;g (2.38&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Black_grouse" title="Black grouse">black grouse</a> (<i>Tetrao tetrix</i>) and the 430&#160;g (15&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Hazel_grouse" title="Hazel grouse">hazel grouse</a> (<i>Bonasa bonasia</i>) followed in numbers by larger 2,950&#160;g (6.50&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Western_capercaillie" title="Western capercaillie">western capercaillies</a> (<i>Tetrao urogallus</i>) and the 570&#160;g (1.26&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Willow_ptarmigan" title="Willow ptarmigan">willow ptarmigan</a> (<i>Lagopus lagopus</i>) which replace the other species in the lower tundra zone. The impression of goshawks on the populations of this prey is considerable, possibly the most impactful of any predator in northern Europe considering their proficiency as predators and similarity of habitat selection to forest grouse. An estimated 25-26% of adult hazel grouses in Finnish and Swedish populations in a few studies fall victim to goshawks, whereas about 14% of adult black grouse are lost to this predator. Lesser numbers were reportedly culled in one study from northern <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>. However, adult grouse are less important in the breeding season diet than young birds, an estimated 30% of grouse taken by Scandinavian goshawks in summer were neonatal chicks whereas 53% were about fledgling age, the remaining 17% being adult grouse.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Widen_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Widen-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tornberg_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tornberg-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> This is fairly different than in southeastern <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, where grouse are similarly as important as in Fennoscandia, as 32.1% of avian prey deliveries were adults, 14.4% were fledglings and 53.5% were nestlings.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_gentilis_f_juv_captive.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Accipiter_gentilis_f_juv_captive.jpg/220px-Accipiter_gentilis_f_juv_captive.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Accipiter_gentilis_f_juv_captive.jpg/330px-Accipiter_gentilis_f_juv_captive.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Accipiter_gentilis_f_juv_captive.jpg/440px-Accipiter_gentilis_f_juv_captive.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1984" data-file-height="1488" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_gentilis_f_juv_captive.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Goshawks sometimes become habitual fowl killers. This juvenile was caught pursuing chickens inside a hen house.</div></div></div>
<p>Northern goshawks can show somewhat of a trend for females to be taken more so than males while hunting adult gamebirds, due to the larger size and more developed defenses of males (such as leg spurs present for defense and innerspecies conflicts in male of most pheasant species). Some authors have claimed this of male <a href="/wiki/Ring-necked_pheasant" class="mw-redirect" title="Ring-necked pheasant">ring-necked pheasant</a> (<i>Phasianus cochilus</i>), but these trends are not reported everywhere, as in southern <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> equal numbers of adult male and female ring-necked pheasants, both sexes averaging 1,135&#160;g (2.502&#160;lb), were taken.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> While male goshawks can take black and hazel grouse of any age and thence deliver them to nests, they can only take capercaillie of up to adult hen size, averaging some 1,800&#160;g (4.0&#160;lb), the cock capercaillie at more than twice as heavy as the hen is too large for a male goshawk to overtake. However, adult female goshawks have been reported attacking and killing cock capercaillie, mainly during winter. These average about 4,000&#160;g (8.8&#160;lb) in body mass and occasionally may weigh even more when dispatched.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-profitability_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-profitability-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Widen_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Widen-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tornberg_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tornberg-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Similarly impressive feats of attacks on other particularly large gamebirds have been reported elsewhere in the range, including the 2,770&#160;g (6.11&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Altai_snowcock" title="Altai snowcock">Altai snowcock</a> (<i>Tetraogallus altaicus</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a> and, in at least one case, successful predation on an estimated 3,900&#160;g (8.6&#160;lb) adult-sized young <a href="/wiki/Wild_turkey" title="Wild turkey">wild turkey</a> (<i>Meleagris gallopavo</i>) hen in <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> (by an immature female goshawk weighing approximately 1,050&#160;g (2.31&#160;lb)), although taking adults of much larger-bodied prey like this is considered generally rare, the young chicks and poults of such prey species are likely much more often taken.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> At the other end of the size scale, the smallest gamebird known to be hunted by northern goshawk was the 96&#160;g (3.4&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Common_quail" title="Common quail">common quail</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mañosa_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mañosa-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Domestic fowl, particularly <a href="/wiki/Chicken" title="Chicken">chickens</a> (<i>Gallus gallus domesticus</i>) are taken occasionally, especially where wild prey populations are depleted. While other raptors are at times blamed for large numbers of attacks on fowl, goshawks are reportedly rather more likely to attack chickens during the day than other raptors and are probably the most habitual avian predator of domestic fowl, at least in the temperate-zone. Particularly large numbers of chickens have been reported in <a href="/wiki/Wigry_National_Park" title="Wigry National Park">Wigry National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> (4th most regular prey species and contributing 15.3% of prey weight), <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, being the third most regularly reported prey in the latter two.<sup id="cite_ref-Rashid_54-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rashid-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zawadzka_107-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zawadzka-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Slisky_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slisky-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ivanovsky_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ivanovsky-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a study of British goshawks, the <a href="/wiki/Red_grouse" title="Red grouse">red grouse</a> (<i>Lagopus lagopus scotica</i>), a race of willow ptarmigan, was found to be the leading prey species (26.2% of prey by number).<sup id="cite_ref-Marquiss_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marquiss-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Segarra" title="Segarra">La Segarra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, the 528&#160;g (1.164&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Red-legged_partridge" title="Red-legged partridge">red-legged partridge</a> (<i>Alectoris rufa</i>) is the most commonly reported prey species (just over 18% by number and 24.5% by weight).<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mañosa_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mañosa-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Despite reports that grouse are less significant as prey to American goshawks, the 560&#160;g (1.23&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Ruffed_grouse" title="Ruffed grouse">ruffed grouse</a> (<i>Bonasa umbellus</i>) is one of the most important prey species in North America (fourth most reported prey species in 22 studies), having been the leading prey species for goshawks in studies from <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a> (from 12 to 25% of prey selected) and reported as taken in high numbers elsewhere in several parts of their mutual range.<sup id="cite_ref-Bosakowski2_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bosakowski2-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Becker_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Becker-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Grzybowski,_J._A._1976_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grzybowski,_J._A._1976-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Graham_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>  The 1,056&#160;g (2.328&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Sooty_grouse" title="Sooty grouse">sooty grouse</a> (<i>Dendragapus fuliginosus</i>) was reported as the leading prey species in southern <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> (28.4% by number).<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lewis2006_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis2006-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> In the boreal forests of <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a>, grouse are fairly important prey especially in winter.<sup id="cite_ref-Schaffer_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schaffer-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Squirrels">Squirrels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Squirrels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p>Among mammalian prey, indisputably the most significant by number are the squirrels. All told, 44 members of the <a href="/wiki/Sciuridae" class="mw-redirect" title="Sciuridae">Sciuridae</a> have turned up in their foods. Tree squirrels are the most obviously co-habitants with goshawks and are indeed taken in high numbers. Alongside <a href="/wiki/Marten" title="Marten">martens</a>, northern goshawks are perhaps the most efficient temperate-zone predators of tree squirrels. Goshawks are large and powerful enough to overtake even the heaviest tree squirrels unlike smaller <i>Accipiters</i> and have greater agility and endurance in pursuits than do most buteonine hawks, some of which like <a href="/wiki/Red-tailed_hawk" title="Red-tailed hawk">red-tailed hawks</a> (<i>Buteo jamaicensis</i>) regularly pursue tree squirrels but have relatively low hunting success rates due to the agility of squirrels.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> The 296&#160;g (10.4&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Red_squirrel" title="Red squirrel">red squirrel</a> (<i>Sciurus vulgaris</i>) of Eurasia is the most numerous mammalian prey in European studies and the sixth most often recorded prey species there overall.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Oulu" title="Oulu">Oulu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> during winter (24.6% by number), in <a href="/wiki/Bia%C5%82owie%C5%BCa_Forest" title="Białowieża Forest">Białowieża Forest</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> (14.3%), in the <a href="/wiki/Ch%C5%99iby" title="Chřiby">Chřiby uplands</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a> (8.5%) and in <a href="/wiki/List_of_forests_in_France" title="List of forests in France">Forêt de Bercé</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>  (12%) the red squirrel was the main prey species for goshawks.<sup id="cite_ref-Białowieża_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Białowieża-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, tree squirrels are even more significant as prey, particularly the modestly-sized <a href="/wiki/Pine_squirrel" title="Pine squirrel">pine squirrels</a> which are the single most important prey type for American goshawks overall. Particularly the 240&#160;g (8.5&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/American_red_squirrel" title="American red squirrel">American red squirrel</a> (<i>Tamiasciurus hudsonicus</i>) is significant, being the primary prey in studies from <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Dakota" title="South Dakota">South Dakota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a> and <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">Montana</a> (in each comprising more than 30% of the diet and present in more than half of known pellets) but also reported everywhere in their foods from the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_United_States" title="Eastern United States">eastern United States</a> to <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>. Much like the <a href="/wiki/American_marten" title="American marten">American marten</a> (<i>Martes americana</i>), the American distribution of goshawks is largely concurrent with that of American red squirrels, indicating the particular significance of it as a dietary staple.<sup id="cite_ref-Smithers2005_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smithers2005-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schaffer_148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schaffer-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Graham_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Squires2_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Squires2-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_northwest" class="mw-redirect" title="Pacific northwest">Pacific northwest</a>, the 165&#160;g (5.8&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Douglas_squirrel" title="Douglas squirrel">Douglas squirrel</a> (<i>Tamiasciurus douglasii</i>) replaces the red squirrel in both distribution and as the highest contributor to goshawk diets from <a href="/wiki/Northern_California" title="Northern California">northern California</a> to <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a>. The largest occurrence of Douglas squirrel known was from <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tahoe" title="Lake Tahoe">Lake Tahoe</a>, where they constituted 23% of prey by number and 32.9% by weight.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Watson_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Promessi_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Promessi-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Larger tree squirrels are also taken opportunistically, in <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a>, the 530&#160;g (1.17&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Eastern_gray_squirrel" title="Eastern gray squirrel">eastern gray squirrel</a> (<i>Sciurus carolinensis</i>) was the third most significant prey species.<sup id="cite_ref-Bosakowski2_131-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bosakowski2-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Becker_132-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Becker-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> Much larger tree squirrels such as <a href="/wiki/Western_gray_squirrel" title="Western gray squirrel">western gray squirrels</a> (<i>Sciurus griseus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Fox_squirrel" title="Fox squirrel">fox squirrels</a> (<i>Sciurus niger</i>), both weighing about 800&#160;g (1.8&#160;lb), are taken occasionally in North America.<sup id="cite_ref-Drennan_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drennan-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Storer_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Storer-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ground_squirrel" title="Ground squirrel">Ground squirrels</a> are also important prey species, mostly in North America, 25 of 44 of squirrel species found in the diet are ground squirrels. Particularly widely reported as a secondary food staple from <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a>, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a> was the 187&#160;g (6.6&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Golden-mantled_ground_squirrel" title="Golden-mantled ground squirrel">golden-mantled ground squirrel</a> (<i>Callospermophilus lateralis</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Schnell_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schnell-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reynolds_96-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rogers_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rogers-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a> and <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a>’s <a href="/wiki/Sawtooth_National_Forest" title="Sawtooth National Forest">Sawtooth National Forest</a>, the 285&#160;g (10.1&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Belding%27s_ground_squirrel" title="Belding&#39;s ground squirrel">Belding's ground squirrel</a> (<i>Urocitellus beldingi</i>) fully dominated the food spectrum, comprising up to 74.3% of the prey by number and 84.2% by biomass.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Miller_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Even much bigger ground squirrels such as <a href="/wiki/Prairie_dog" title="Prairie dog">prairie dogs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marmot" title="Marmot">marmots</a> are attacked on occasion.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> Several <a href="/wiki/Hoary_marmot" title="Hoary marmot">hoary marmots</a> (<i>Marmota caligala</i>) were brought to nests in southeast Alaska but averaged only 1,894&#160;g (4.176&#160;lb), so were young animals about half of the average adult (spring) weight (albeit still considerably heavier than the goshawks who took them).<sup id="cite_ref-Lewis2006_102-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis2006-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> In some cases, adult marmots such as <a href="/wiki/Alpine_marmot" title="Alpine marmot">alpine marmots</a> (<i>Marmota marmota</i>), <a href="/wiki/Yellow-bellied_marmot" title="Yellow-bellied marmot">yellow-bellied marmots</a> (<i>Marmota flaviventris</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Woodchuck" class="mw-redirect" title="Woodchuck">woodchucks</a> (<i>Marmota monax</i>) have been preyed upon when lighter and weaker in spring, collectively weighing on average about 3,500&#160;g (7.7&#160;lb) or about three times as much as a female goshawk although are basically half of what these marmots can weigh by fall.<sup id="cite_ref-Becker_132-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Becker-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Perrone_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Perrone-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> About a dozen species of <a href="/wiki/Chipmunk" title="Chipmunk">chipmunk</a> are known to be taken by goshawks and the 96&#160;g (3.4&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Eastern_chipmunk" title="Eastern chipmunk">eastern chipmunks</a> (<i>Tamias striatus</i>) were the second most numerous prey species at nests in central <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a> and <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Smithers2005_100-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smithers2005-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-White_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-White-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Grzybowski,_J._A._1976_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grzybowski,_J._A._1976-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Squirrels taken have ranged in size from the 43&#160;g (1.5&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Least_chipmunk" title="Least chipmunk">least chipmunk</a> (<i>Tamias minimus</i>) to the aforementioned adult marmots.<sup id="cite_ref-Perrone_169-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Perrone-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hares_and_rabbits">Hares and rabbits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Hares and rabbits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Rabbit_(1898)_%27Just_missed_him%27.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/The_Rabbit_%281898%29_%27Just_missed_him%27.png/220px-The_Rabbit_%281898%29_%27Just_missed_him%27.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="131" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/The_Rabbit_%281898%29_%27Just_missed_him%27.png/330px-The_Rabbit_%281898%29_%27Just_missed_him%27.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/The_Rabbit_%281898%29_%27Just_missed_him%27.png/440px-The_Rabbit_%281898%29_%27Just_missed_him%27.png 2x" data-file-width="532" data-file-height="317" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Rabbit_(1898)_%27Just_missed_him%27.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustrating a goshawk attempting to catch a <a href="/wiki/European_rabbit" title="European rabbit">rabbit</a>, by <a href="/wiki/George_Edward_Lodge" title="George Edward Lodge">G. E. Lodge</a></div></div></div>
<p>Northern goshawks can be locally heavy predators of <a href="/wiki/Lagomorph" class="mw-redirect" title="Lagomorph">lagomorphs</a>, of which they take at least 15 species as prey. Especially in the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Iberian peninsula">Iberian peninsula</a>, the native <a href="/wiki/European_rabbit" title="European rabbit">European rabbit</a> (<i>Oryctolagus cuniculus</i>) is often delivered to nests and can be the most numerous prey. Even where taken secondarily in numbers in Spain to gamebirds such as in <a href="/wiki/Segarra" title="Segarra">La Segarra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, rabbits tend to be the most significant contributor of biomass to goshawk nests. On average, the weight of rabbits taken in La Segarra was 662&#160;g (1.459&#160;lb) (making up 38.4% of the prey biomass there), indicating most of the 333 rabbits taken there were yearlings and about 2-3 times lighter than a prime adult wild rabbit.<sup id="cite_ref-Mañosa_141-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mañosa-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zuberogoitia_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zuberogoitia-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, where the European rabbit is an introduced species, it was the third most numerous prey species at nests.<sup id="cite_ref-Marquiss_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marquiss-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> In more snowbound areas where wild and feral rabbits are absent, larger <a href="/wiki/Hare" title="Hare">hares</a> may be taken and while perhaps more difficult to subdue than most typical goshawk prey, are a highly nutritious food source. In <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, females were found to take <a href="/wiki/Mountain_hare" title="Mountain hare">mountain hare</a> (<i>Lepus timidus</i>) fairly often and they were the second most numerous prey item for goshawks in winter (14.8% by number).<sup id="cite_ref-Tornberg_135-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tornberg-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, where mammals are more important in the diet, more lagomorphs are taken. In <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Snowshoe_hare" title="Snowshoe hare">snowshoe hares</a> (<i>Lepus americanus</i>) are the largest contributor of biomass to goshawks foods (making up to 36.6% of the prey by weight), in eastern Oregon at least 60% of hares taken were adults weighing on average 1,500&#160;g (3.3&#160;lb), and in one of three studies from Oregon be the most numerous prey species (second most numerous in the other two).<sup id="cite_ref-Reynolds_96-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Watson_121-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> This species was also the second most numerous food species in Alberta throughout the year and the most important prey by weight.<sup id="cite_ref-Schaffer_148-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schaffer-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eastern_cottontail" title="Eastern cottontail">Eastern cottontails</a> (<i>Sylvilagus floridanus</i>), also averaging some 1,500&#160;g (3.3&#160;lb) in mass per the study (and thus mostly consisting of adult cottontails in their prime), were the most significant prey both by weight (42.3%) and number (13.3%) in <a href="/wiki/Apache-Sitgreaves_National_Forest" class="mw-redirect" title="Apache-Sitgreaves National Forest">Apache-Sitgreaves National Forest</a> of <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>. Eastern cottontails are also taken regularly in <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Meng_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meng-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rogers_164-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rogers-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> In some parts of the range, larger leporids may be attacked, extending to the 2,410&#160;g (5.31&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Black-tailed_jackrabbit" title="Black-tailed jackrabbit">black-tailed</a> (<i>Lepus californicus</i>) and the 3,200&#160;g (7.1&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/White-tailed_jackrabbit" title="White-tailed jackrabbit">white-tailed jackrabbit</a> (<i>Lepus townsendii</i>), the  3,800&#160;g (8.4&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/European_hare" title="European hare">European hares</a> (<i>Lepus europaeus</i>), as well as the mountain hare.<sup id="cite_ref-Drennan_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drennan-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> In Europe, males have been recorded successfully attacking rabbits weighing up to 1,600&#160;g (3.5&#160;lb), or about 2.2 times their own weight, while adult mountain hares overtaken by female goshawks in Fennoscandia have weighed from 2,700 to 3,627&#160;g (5.952 to 7.996&#160;lb) or up to 2.4 times their own weight. Despite historic claims that taking prey so considerably larger than themselves is exceptional beyond a small region of <a href="/wiki/Fennoscandia" title="Fennoscandia">Fennoscandia</a>, there is evidence that as grouse numbers have mysteriously declined since 1960, adult mountain hare are increasingly the leading prey for wintering female goshawks, favoring and causing an increase of larger bodied females in order to overpower such a substantial catch.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kenward2_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward2-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Asian and American goshawks also take about a half dozen species of <a href="/wiki/Pika" title="Pika">pikas</a>, much smaller cousins of rabbits and hares, but they are at best supplementary prey for American goshawks and of unknown importance to little-studied Asian populations.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_121-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_birds">Other birds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Other birds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Colaptes_auratus_FWS.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Colaptes_auratus_FWS.jpg/220px-Colaptes_auratus_FWS.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="272" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Colaptes_auratus_FWS.jpg/330px-Colaptes_auratus_FWS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Colaptes_auratus_FWS.jpg/440px-Colaptes_auratus_FWS.jpg 2x" data-file-width="533" data-file-height="658" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Colaptes_auratus_FWS.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Woodpecker" title="Woodpecker">Woodpeckers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Northern_flicker" title="Northern flicker">northern flickers</a> often fall victim to goshawks</div></div></div>
<p>Some 21 species of <a href="/wiki/Woodpecker" title="Woodpecker">woodpecker</a> have been reported from northern goshawk food studies around the world. With their relatively slow, undulating flight adult and fledged woodpeckers can easily be overtaken by hunting goshawks, not to mention their habitat preferences frequently put them within active goshawk ranges. Most of the widespread species from Europe and North America have been observed as prey, most commonly relatively large woodpeckers such as the 76&#160;g (2.7&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Greater_spotted_woodpecker" class="mw-redirect" title="Greater spotted woodpecker">greater spotted woodpecker</a> (<i>Dendrocopos major</i>) and the 176&#160;g (6.2&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/European_green_woodpecker" title="European green woodpecker">European green woodpecker</a> (<i>Picus viridis</i>) in Europe and the 134&#160;g (4.7&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Northern_flicker" title="Northern flicker">northern flicker</a> (<i>Colaptes auratus</i>) in North America.
