Indeed, the flicker is the third most regularly reported prey species in America.<sup id="cite_ref-Zawadzka_107-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zawadzka-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schreven_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schreven-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> In south-central <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a>, the northern flicker was the second most numerous prey species and it was the main prey species in a study from <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a> (here making up 26.4% of prey by number).<sup id="cite_ref-Squires2_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Squires2-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> All sizes of woodpeckers available are taken from the 19.8&#160;g (0.70&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Lesser_spotted_woodpecker" title="Lesser spotted woodpecker">lesser spotted woodpecker</a> (<i>Dryobates minor</i>) to the 321&#160;g (11.3&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Black_woodpecker" title="Black woodpecker">black woodpecker</a> (<i>Dryocopus martius</i>) in Europe and from the 25.6&#160;g (0.90&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Downy_woodpecker" title="Downy woodpecker">downy woodpecker</a> (<i>Picoides pubescens</i>) to the 287&#160;g (10.1&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Pileated_woodpecker" title="Pileated woodpecker">pileated woodpecker</a> (<i>Dryocopus pileatus</i>) in North America.<sup id="cite_ref-Zawadzka_107-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zawadzka-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schaffer_148-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schaffer-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Promessi_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Promessi-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> In many areas, northern goshawks will pursue <a href="/wiki/Water_bird" title="Water bird">water birds</a> of several varieties, although they rarely form a large portion of the diet. Perhaps the most often recorded water birds in the diet are <a href="/wiki/Duck" title="Duck">ducks</a>. All told, 32 waterfowl have been recorded in their diet.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Ural_mountain" class="mw-redirect" title="Ural mountain">Ural mountains</a>, the nearly cosmopolitan 1,075&#160;g (2.370&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Mallard" title="Mallard">mallard</a> (<i>Anas platyrhynchos</i>) was third most numerous prey species.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> The ducks of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Aythya" title="Aythya">Aythya</a></i> are somewhat frequently recorded as well, especially since their tree-nesting habits may frequently put them in the hunting range of nesting goshawks.<sup id="cite_ref-Smithers2005_100-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smithers2005-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lewis2006_102-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis2006-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, the <a href="/wiki/Wood_duck" title="Wood duck">wood duck</a> (<i>Aix sponsa</i>) from America and the <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_duck" title="Mandarin duck">mandarin duck</a> (<i>Aix galericulata</i>) from Asia may be more vulnerable than most waterfowl at their tree nests.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> Although etymologists feel that the goshawk is an abbreviation of “goose-hawk”, geese are seldomly taken considering their generally much larger size. Nonetheless, four species have been taken, including adults of species as large as the 2,420&#160;g (5.34&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Greater_white-fronted_goose" title="Greater white-fronted goose">greater white-fronted goose</a> (<i>Anser albifrons</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Veldkamp_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Veldkamp-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Møller_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Møller-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> Adult <a href="/wiki/Common_eider" title="Common eider">common eiders</a> (<i>Somateria mollissima</i>), the largest northern duck at 2,066&#160;g (4.555&#160;lb), have also been captured by goshawks.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> Various other water birds reported as taken include <a href="/wiki/Red-throated_loon" title="Red-throated loon">red-throated loon</a> (<i>Gavia stellata</i>) chicks, adult <a href="/wiki/Little_grebe" title="Little grebe">little grebes</a> (<i>Tachybaptus ruficollis</i>), adult <a href="/wiki/Great_cormorant" title="Great cormorant">great cormorants</a> (<i>Phalacrocorax carbo</i>) (about the same size as a greater white-fronted goose), adult <a href="/wiki/Crested_ibis" title="Crested ibis">crested ibis</a> (<i>Nipponia nippon</i>), <a href="/wiki/Black_stork" title="Black stork">black stork</a> (<i>Ciconia nigra</i>) chicks and five species each of <a href="/wiki/Heron" title="Heron">heron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rail_(bird)" title="Rail (bird)">rail</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Opdam_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Opdam-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Veldkamp_186-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Veldkamp-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> Among <a href="/wiki/Shorebird" class="mw-redirect" title="Shorebird">shorebirds</a> (or small waders), goshawks have been reported preying on more than 22 <a href="/wiki/Sandpiper" title="Sandpiper">sandpipers</a>, more than 8 <a href="/wiki/Plover" title="Plover">plovers</a>, more than 10 species each of <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">gull</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tern" title="Tern">tern</a>, more than 2 species of <a href="/wiki/Alcid" class="mw-redirect" title="Alcid">alcids</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_stone-curlew" title="Eurasian stone-curlew">Eurasian stone-curlew</a> (<i>Burhinus oedicnemus</i>), the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_oystercatcher" title="Eurasian oystercatcher">Eurasian oystercatcher</a> (<i>Haematopus ostralegus</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Long-tailed_jaeger" title="Long-tailed jaeger">long-tailed jaeger</a> (<i>Stercorarius longicaudus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Verdal_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Verdal-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Becker_132-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Becker-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ivanovsky_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ivanovsky-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Møller_189-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Møller-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Belika_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Belika-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%E3%82%AA%E3%82%AA%E3%82%BF%E3%82%AB%E3%81%AE%E3%83%92%E3%83%8A.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/%E3%82%AA%E3%82%AA%E3%82%BF%E3%82%AB%E3%81%AE%E3%83%92%E3%83%8A.jpg/220px-%E3%82%AA%E3%82%AA%E3%82%BF%E3%82%AB%E3%81%AE%E3%83%92%E3%83%8A.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/%E3%82%AA%E3%82%AA%E3%82%BF%E3%82%AB%E3%81%AE%E3%83%92%E3%83%8A.jpg/330px-%E3%82%AA%E3%82%AA%E3%82%BF%E3%82%AB%E3%81%AE%E3%83%92%E3%83%8A.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/%E3%82%AA%E3%82%AA%E3%82%BF%E3%82%AB%E3%81%AE%E3%83%92%E3%83%8A.jpg/440px-%E3%82%AA%E3%82%AA%E3%82%BF%E3%82%AB%E3%81%AE%E3%83%92%E3%83%8A.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1498" data-file-height="938" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%E3%82%AA%E3%82%AA%E3%82%BF%E3%82%AB%E3%81%AE%E3%83%92%E3%83%8A.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Juvenile in Japan with a young bird prey item</div></div></div>
<p>Corvids as aforementioned are quite important prey. Although they take fewer passerines than other northern <i>Accipiters</i>, smaller types of songbirds can still be regionally important to the diet. This is especially true of the <a href="/wiki/Thrush_(bird)" title="Thrush (bird)">thrushes</a> which are often delivered to nests in Europe. 17 species of thrush have been identified in goshawk food across their range. The numerous 103&#160;g (3.6&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_blackbird" class="mw-redirect" title="Eurasian blackbird">Eurasian blackbird</a> (<i>Turdus merula</i>) is often most reported from this family and can even be the main prey at some locations such as in the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> (23.5% of prey by number) and in <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> (just over 14% by number and two studies showed thrushes collectively make up nearly half of the prey items in Norwegian nests).<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Verdal_114-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Verdal-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schreven_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schreven-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> All common <i><a href="/wiki/Turdus" class="mw-redirect" title="Turdus">Turdus</a></i> species are taken in some numbers in Europe, being quite regular and conspicuous in the woodland edge zones most often patrolled by male goshawks, especially while singing in spring and summer. Even where larger, more nutritious prey is present such as at pheasant release sites, the abundant thrushes are more often delivered to the nest because of the ease of capture such as in <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Verdal_114-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Verdal-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kenward2_178-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward2-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> Smaller numbers of thrush are taken in general in North America but the 78&#160;g (2.8&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/American_robin" title="American robin">American robin</a> (<i>Turdus migratorius</i>) are fairly regular prey nonetheless and were the most numerous prey in the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Nevada_(U.S.)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sierra Nevada (U.S.)">Sierra Nevadas</a> of <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> (30.7% by number and 21.4% by weight).<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schnell_34-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schnell-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Miller_166-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Thrush taken have ranged in size from the 26.4&#160;g (0.93&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Western_bluebird" title="Western bluebird">western bluebird</a> (<i>Sialia mexicana</i>), the smallest bluebird and lightest North American thrush on average, to the 118&#160;g (4.2&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Mistle_thrush" title="Mistle thrush">mistle thrush</a> (<i>Turdus viscivorus</i>), Europe's largest thrush.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Drennan_161-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drennan-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Penteriani_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penteriani-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Beyond corvids and thrushes, most passerines encountered by northern goshawks are substantially smaller and are often ignored under most circumstances in favor of more sizable prey. Nonetheless, more than a hundred passerines have been recorded their diet beyond these families. Most widespread passerine families from North America and Europe have occasional losses to goshawks, including <a href="/wiki/Tyrant_flycatcher" title="Tyrant flycatcher">tyrant flycatchers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shrike" title="Shrike">shrikes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vireo" title="Vireo">vireos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lark" title="Lark">larks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swallow" title="Swallow">swallows</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuthatch" title="Nuthatch">nuthatches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Treecreeper" title="Treecreeper">treecreepers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_wren" title="Eurasian wren">wrens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mimid" title="Mimid">mimids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_World_warbler" title="Old World warbler">Old World warblers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_World_flycatcher" title="Old World flycatcher">Old World flycatchers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pipit" title="Pipit">pipits</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wagtail" title="Wagtail">wagtails</a>, <a href="/wiki/Starling" title="Starling">starlings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Waxwing" title="Waxwing">waxwings</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_World_warbler" title="New World warbler">New World warblers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emberizidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Emberizidae">emberizine sparrows</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_(bird)" title="Cardinal (bird)">cardinalids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Icterid" title="Icterid">icterids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finch" title="Finch">finches</a> and <a href="/wiki/Old_World_sparrow" title="Old World sparrow">Old World sparrows</a>. Avian prey has even ranged to as small as the 5.5&#160;g (0.19&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Goldcrest" title="Goldcrest">goldcrest</a> (<i>Regulus regulus</i>), the smallest bird in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zawadzka_107-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zawadzka-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> In North America, the smallest known bird prey is the 8.2&#160;g (0.29&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/American_redstart" title="American redstart">American redstart</a> (<i>Setophaga ruticilla</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Becker_132-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Becker-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Among smaller types of passerines, one of the most widely reported are <a href="/wiki/Finch" title="Finch">finches</a> and, in some widespread studies, somewhat substantial numbers of finches of many species may actually be taken. Finches tend to fly more conspicuously as they cover longer distances, often bounding or undulating as they do, over the canopy than most forest songbirds, which may make them more susceptible to goshawk attacks than other small songbirds.<sup id="cite_ref-Møller_189-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Møller-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bezzel_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bezzel-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> Non-passerine upland birds taken by goshawks in small numbers include but are not limited to <a href="/wiki/Nightjar" title="Nightjar">nightjars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swift" title="Swift">swifts</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_bee-eater" title="European bee-eater">bee-eaters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingfisher" title="Kingfisher">kingfishers</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_roller" title="European roller">rollers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hoopoe" title="Hoopoe">hoopoes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parrot" title="Parrot">parrots</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Zawadzka_107-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zawadzka-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mañosa_141-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mañosa-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Abuladze_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abuladze-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Møller_189-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Møller-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_mammals">Other mammals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Other mammals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Outside of the squirrel family, relatively few other types of <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a> are taken in many regions. In eastern <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a>, the 132&#160;g (4.7&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Northern_flying_squirrel" title="Northern flying squirrel">northern flying squirrel</a> (<i>Glaucomys sabrinus</i>) (flying squirrels are not true squirrels) was the third or fourth most frequently caught prey species.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_121-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reynolds_96-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cricetidae" title="Cricetidae">Microtine rodents</a> which are so essential to most northern non-accipiter hawks and a majority of owls are at best a secondary contributor to goshawk diets, even though 26 species have been reported in their diet. Exceptionally, in a study of the <a href="/wiki/Carpathian_mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Carpathian mountains">Carpathian mountains</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, the 27.5&#160;g (0.97&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Common_vole" title="Common vole">common vole</a> (<i>Microtus arvalis</i>) was the second most numerous prey species.<sup id="cite_ref-White_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-White-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Slisky_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slisky-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Relatively high numbers of the 18.4&#160;g (0.65&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Bank_vole" title="Bank vole">bank vole</a> (<i>Myodes glareolus</i>) were reported in diets from <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> in <a href="/wiki/Gmina_Sob%C3%B3tka" title="Gmina Sobótka">Gmina Sobótka</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bia%C5%82owie%C5%BCa_Forest" title="Białowieża Forest">Białowieża Forest</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Białowieża_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Białowieża-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gryz_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gryz-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> During summer in <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a>, the 44&#160;g (1.6&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Meadow_vole" title="Meadow vole">meadow vole</a> (<i>Microtus pennsylvanicus</i>) was the third most frequently reported prey species, the only known study where large numbers of microtine rodents were taken in North America.<sup id="cite_ref-Schaffer_148-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schaffer-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> Microtine rodents taken by goshawks have ranged in size from the 11&#160;g (0.39&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Western_harvest_mouse" title="Western harvest mouse">western harvest mouse</a> (<i>Reithrodontomys megalotis</i>) to the 1,105&#160;g (2.436&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Muskrat" title="Muskrat">muskrat</a> (<i>Ondatra zibethicus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Graham_146-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zawadzka_107-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zawadzka-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> Other miscellaneous rodents reported sporadically in the diet include <a href="/wiki/Dormouse" title="Dormouse">dormice</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_American_porcupine" title="North American porcupine">porcupines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ord%27s_kangaroo_rat" title="Ord&#39;s kangaroo rat">kangaroo rats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mountain_beaver" title="Mountain beaver">mountain beavers</a> (<i>Aplodontia rufa</i>), <a href="/wiki/Western_jumping_mouse" title="Western jumping mouse">jumping mice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muridae" title="Muridae">Old World mice and rats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eospalax" title="Eospalax">zokors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gopher" title="Gopher">gophers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Persian_jird" title="Persian jird">jirds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Penteriani_111-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penteriani-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_116-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Watson_121-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Miller_166-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Insectivore" title="Insectivore">Insectivores</a> are taken in low numbers including <a href="/wiki/Mole_(animal)" title="Mole (animal)">moles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shrew" title="Shrew">shrews</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_white-breasted_hedgehog" title="Southern white-breasted hedgehog">hedgehogs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_121-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Białowieża_151-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Białowieża-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zawadzka_107-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zawadzka-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mañosa_141-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mañosa-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sherrod_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sherrod-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> The smallest mammalian prey species known to be attacked by goshawks was the 3.65&#160;g (0.129&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Masked_shrew" class="mw-redirect" title="Masked shrew">masked shrew</a> (<i>Sorex cinereus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_121-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Even more sporadically attacked by goshawks, given this prey's nocturnal habits, are <a href="/wiki/Bat" title="Bat">bats</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> In one case a juvenile <a href="/wiki/Golden_snub-nosed_monkey" title="Golden snub-nosed monkey">golden snub-nosed monkey</a> (<i>Rhinopithecus roxellana</i>), which was successfully taken by a goshawk.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Even-toed_ungulate" title="Even-toed ungulate">Ungulates</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Deer" title="Deer">deer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">sheep</a> are sometimes consumed by goshawks but there is no evidence that they prey on live ones (as much larger accipitrids such as <a href="/wiki/Eagle" title="Eagle">eagles</a> can sometimes do), but these are more likely rare cases of scavenging on carrion, which may more regularly occur than once thought in areas with harsh winter weather.<sup id="cite_ref-Squires_19-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Squires-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gryz_203-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gryz-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sherrod_210-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sherrod-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternate_prey">Alternate prey</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Alternate prey">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In a few cases, northern goshawks have been recorded hunting and killing prey beyond birds and mammals. In some of the warmer drier extensions of their range, <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a> may be available to them to hunt. Only one species of <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">snake</a> is recorded from their diet, the small innocuous <a href="/wiki/Grass_snake" title="Grass snake">grass snake</a> (<i>Natrix natrix</i>), at 66&#160;g (2.3&#160;oz); however about a half dozen <a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">lizards</a> are recorded in their diet, primarily from the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Iberian peninsula">Iberian peninsula</a> but also from the <a href="/wiki/Ural_mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Ural mountains">Ural mountains</a> and the <a href="/wiki/American_southwest" class="mw-redirect" title="American southwest">American southwest</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_116-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Skilsky_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Skilsky-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mañosa_141-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mañosa-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rogers_164-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rogers-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> The only known location in the northern goshawk's range where reptiles were taken in large numbers was <a href="/wiki/Sierra_de_Guadarrama" title="Sierra de Guadarrama">Sierra de Guadarrama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, where the 77&#160;g (2.7&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Ocellated_lizard" title="Ocellated lizard">ocellated lizard</a> (<i>Timon lepidus</i>) was the second most numerous prey species.<sup id="cite_ref-Araujo_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Araujo-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">Amphibians</a> are even rarer in the diet, only recorded more than singly in one study each from <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> and from <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Marquiss_144-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marquiss-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zuberogoitia_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zuberogoitia-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">Fish</a> are similarly rare in the diet, recorded twice each in <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">Bavaria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ivanovsky_143-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ivanovsky-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bezzel_198-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bezzel-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> A few pellets have included remains of <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>, much of which may be ingested incidentally or via the stomachs of birds that they have consumed. However, there is some evidence they at times will hunt large ground-dwelling insects such as <a href="/wiki/Dung_beetle" title="Dung beetle">dung beetles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Opdam_125-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Opdam-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gryz_203-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gryz-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Thiollay_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thiollay-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interspecies_predatory_relationships">Interspecies predatory relationships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Interspecies predatory relationships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_gentilis_chasing_Pandion_haliaetus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Accipiter_gentilis_chasing_Pandion_haliaetus.jpg/220px-Accipiter_gentilis_chasing_Pandion_haliaetus.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Accipiter_gentilis_chasing_Pandion_haliaetus.jpg/330px-Accipiter_gentilis_chasing_Pandion_haliaetus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Accipiter_gentilis_chasing_Pandion_haliaetus.jpg/440px-Accipiter_gentilis_chasing_Pandion_haliaetus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1651" data-file-height="1230" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_gentilis_chasing_Pandion_haliaetus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chasing an <a href="/wiki/Osprey" title="Osprey">osprey</a>, most likely to <a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasitism" title="Kleptoparasitism">rob</a> it of food, but the osprey is even considered possible prey</div></div></div>
<p>Northern goshawks are often near the top of the avian food chain in forested biomes but face competition for food resources from various other predators, including both birds and mammals. Comparative dietary studies have shown that mean sizes of prey, both in terms of its size relative to the raptor itself and absolute weight, for goshawks is relatively larger than in most <a href="/wiki/Buteoninae" title="Buteoninae">buteonine hawks</a> in North America and Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Marti_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> Studies show even buteonine hawks slightly larger than goshawks on average take prey weighing less than 200&#160;g (7.1&#160;oz) whereas average goshawk prey is usually well over such a mass. This is due largely to the much higher importance of microtine rodents to most buteonine hawks, which, despite their occasional abundance, are ignored by goshawks in most regions.<sup id="cite_ref-Marti_217-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, mean prey mass for <a href="/wiki/Sharp-shinned_hawk" title="Sharp-shinned hawk">sharp-shinned</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cooper%27s_hawk" title="Cooper&#39;s hawk">Cooper's hawks</a> in North America is between about 10 and 30% of their own mass, whereas the mean prey of American goshawks is between about 25 and 50% of their own mass and therefore are the goshawks takes prey that is on average relatively much larger.<sup id="cite_ref-Reynolds_96-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Storer_162-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Storer-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> In many of the ecosystems that they inhabit, northern goshawks compete with resources with other predators, particularly where they take sizeable numbers of lagomorphs. About a dozen mammalian and avian predators in each area all primarily consume <a href="/wiki/European_rabbit" title="European rabbit">European rabbits</a> and <a href="/wiki/Snowshoe_hare" title="Snowshoe hare">snowshoe hares</a> alongside goshawks in the Iberian peninsula and the American boreal forest regions where these became primary staple foods. Like those co-habitant predators, the goshawk suffers declines during the low portion in the lagomorph's breeding cycles, which rise and fall cyclically every 10 to 12 years. However, even where these are primary food sources, the northern goshawk is less specialized than many (even <i><a href="/wiki/Horned_owl" title="Horned owl">Bubo</a></i> owls, some of the more generalist avian predators become extremely specialized lagomorph hunters locally, to a greater extent than goshawks) and can alternate their food selection, often taking equal or greater numbers of tree squirrels and woodland birds. Due to this dietary variation, the northern goshawk is less affected than other raptorial birds by prey population cycles and tends to not be depleted by resource competition.<sup id="cite_ref-Doyle_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doyle-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite their propensity to pursue relatively large prey and capability to pursue alternate prey, northern goshawks can be locally outcompeted for resources by species that are more adaptable and flexible, especially in terms of habitat and prey. Most northern buteonine hawks largely take small rodents such as <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">voles</a> (which are usually ignored by goshawks) but can adapt to nearly any other type of prey when the staple local rodent prey populations go down.<sup id="cite_ref-Marti_217-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> Comparisons with goshawks and <a href="/wiki/Red-tailed_hawk" title="Red-tailed hawk">red-tailed hawk</a> nesting in abutting areas of <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a> (other large common <i>Buteos</i> like <a href="/wiki/Swainson%27s_hawk" title="Swainson&#39;s hawk">Swainson's hawks</a> (<i>Buteo swainsonii</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Ferruginous_hawk" title="Ferruginous hawk">ferruginous hawks</a> (<i>Buteo regalis</i>) utilize open habitats and so do not come into conflict with goshawks) shows the red-tailed hawks as being able to take a broader range of prey than goshawks and nest in more varied habitats, the latter species being perhaps the most commonly seen, widespread and adaptable of diurnal American raptors.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> On occasion, goshawks are robbed of their prey by a diversity of other birds, including <a href="/wiki/Harrier_(bird)" title="Harrier (bird)">harriers</a>, other <a href="/wiki/Hawk" title="Hawk">hawks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eagle" title="Eagle">eagles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Falcon" title="Falcon">falcons</a> and even <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">gulls</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ponitz_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ponitz-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Moshkin_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moshkin-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Karlsen_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karlsen-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Northern goshawks from North America are less prone to nesting outside of mature forests and take larger numbers of mammals as opposed to abundant birds than in Europe. This may be in part due to heavier competition from a greater diversity of raptors in North America. In <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, the goshawk only co-exists with the much smaller sparrowhawk within its own genus, while in North America, it lives with the intermediately-sized <a href="/wiki/Cooper%27s_hawk" title="Cooper&#39;s hawk">Cooper's hawk</a>. The latter species much more readily nests in semi-open and developed areas of North America than goshawks there and hunts a broad assemblage of medium-sized birds, whereas such prey is more readily available to male goshawks from Europe than to goshawks in North America. Although the Cooper's hawk usually avoids and loses individual contests against the larger goshawk, its adaptability has allowed it to become the most widespread and commonly found North American <i>Accipiter</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Deforestation_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deforestation-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wight_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wight-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> The slightly larger goshawks of Europe have been shown, in some but not all areas, to outcompete and possibly lower productivity of the slightly smaller <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">common buzzard</a> (<i>Buteo buteo</i>) when their ranges overlap. Usually, however, the dietary habits and nesting preferences are sufficiently distinct and thus effect neither buzzard or goshawk populations. Both can mutually be very common even when the other is present.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Looft_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Looft-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> On the other hand, American goshawks are slightly smaller on average than their European counterparts and can be up to 10% smaller in mass than red-tailed hawks. However, studies have indicated that the goshawk has, beyond its superior speed and agility, has stronger feet and a more forceful attack than that of the red-tailed hawk. All in all, individual competitions between red-tailed hawks and goshawks can go either way and neither is strongly likely to deter the other from nesting given their distinct nesting habitats.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> Other raptors, including most medium to large-sized owls as well as red-tailed hawks and falcons, will use nests built by northern goshawks, even when goshawks are still in the area.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bulletin_-_United_States_National_Museum_(1883)_(14596415950).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Bulletin_-_United_States_National_Museum_%281883%29_%2814596415950%29.jpg/220px-Bulletin_-_United_States_National_Museum_%281883%29_%2814596415950%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="122" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Bulletin_-_United_States_National_Museum_%281883%29_%2814596415950%29.jpg/330px-Bulletin_-_United_States_National_Museum_%281883%29_%2814596415950%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Bulletin_-_United_States_National_Museum_%281883%29_%2814596415950%29.jpg/440px-Bulletin_-_United_States_National_Museum_%281883%29_%2814596415950%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1284" data-file-height="714" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bulletin_-_United_States_National_Museum_(1883)_(14596415950).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of the formidable talons and beak, which are both proportionately large relative to their size, and give them a predatory advantage over many other raptors</div></div></div>
<p>To many other raptorial birds, the northern goshawk is more significant as a predatory threat than as competition. The northern goshawk is one of the most dangerous species to other raptors, especially to those considerably smaller than itself. In many cases, raptors of any age from nestlings to adults are taken around their nests but free-flying raptors too are readily taken or ambushed at a perch.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sergio_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sergio-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lourenço_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lourenço-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> One example is a study from <a href="/wiki/Northern_England" title="Northern England">northern England</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Common_kestrel" title="Common kestrel">common kestrels</a> (<i>Falco tinnunculus</i>), which average about 184&#160;g (6.5&#160;oz), recorded as prey at goshawk nests (mainly in March and April) numbered 139, a larger number than kestrels recorded alive in the spring in the same area.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Petty_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petty-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Veluwe" title="Veluwe">Veluwe province</a> of <a href="/wiki/The_Netherlands" class="mw-redirect" title="The Netherlands">the Netherlands</a>, the percentage of nest of <a href="/wiki/European_honey_buzzard" title="European honey buzzard">European honey buzzards</a> (<i>Pernis apivorus</i>), weighing on average 760&#160;g (1.68&#160;lb), predated by goshawks increased from a little as 7.7% in 1981–1990 to 33% in 2000–2004.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> As their habitat preferences may overlap with goshawks, all other <i>Accipiters</i> encountered may be predated in multiple cases, including the 238&#160;g (8.4&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_sparrowhawk" title="Eurasian sparrowhawk">Eurasian sparrowhawk</a>, the 188&#160;g (6.6&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Levant_sparrowhawk" title="Levant sparrowhawk">levant sparrowhawk</a> (<i>Accipiter brevipes</i>), the 136&#160;g (4.8&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Sharp-shinned_hawk" title="Sharp-shinned hawk">sharp-shinned hawk</a>, the 122&#160;g (4.3&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Japanese_sparrowhawk" title="Japanese sparrowhawk">Japanese sparrowhawk</a> (<i>Accipiter gularis</i>) and the 440&#160;g (0.97&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Cooper%27s_hawk" title="Cooper&#39;s hawk">Cooper's hawk</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Becker_132-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Becker-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lewis2006_102-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis2006-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Belika_193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Belika-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Penteriani_111-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penteriani-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Other assorted accipitrids of up to their own size to be predated by goshawks include the 747&#160;g (1.647&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Black_kite" title="Black kite">black kite</a> (<i>Milvus migrans</i>), the 1,080&#160;g (2.38&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Red_kite" title="Red kite">red kite</a> (<i>Milvus milvus</i>), the 712&#160;g (1.570&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Western_marsh-harrier" class="mw-redirect" title="Western marsh-harrier">western marsh-harrier</a> (<i>Circus aeruginosus</i>), the 316&#160;g (11.1&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Montagu%27s_harrier" title="Montagu&#39;s harrier">Montagu's harrier</a> (<i>Circus pygargus</i>), the 390&#160;g (14&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Pallid_harrier" title="Pallid harrier">pallid harrier</a> (<i>Circus macrourus</i>) and <i>Buteos</i> of up to adults including the 776&#160;g (1.711&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">common buzzard</a>, the 424&#160;g (15.0&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Broad-winged_hawk" title="Broad-winged hawk">broad-winged hawk</a> (<i>Buteo platypterus</i>), the 610&#160;g (1.34&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Red-shouldered_hawk" title="Red-shouldered hawk">red-shouldered hawk</a> (<i>Buteo lineatus</i>) and the 1,065&#160;g (2.348&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Red-tailed_hawk" title="Red-tailed hawk">red-tailed hawk</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reynolds_96-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smithers2005_100-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smithers2005-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Penteriani_111-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penteriani-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ivanovsky_143-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ivanovsky-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Thiollay_215-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thiollay-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Arizona_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arizona-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-János_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-János-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> Even raptors somewhat larger than a northern goshawks have been considered as prey, although it is not clear whether adults are among the victims, including the 1,494&#160;g (3.294&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Osprey" title="Osprey">osprey</a> (<i>Pandion haliaetus</i>), 1,147&#160;g (2.529&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Crested_honey-buzzard" class="mw-redirect" title="Crested honey-buzzard">crested honey-buzzard</a> (<i>Pernis ptilorhynchus</i>) and the 1,370&#160;g (3.02&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Lesser_spotted_eagle" title="Lesser spotted eagle">lesser spotted eagle</a> (<i>Clanga pomarina</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-János_248-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-János-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Outside of the accipitrid group, heavy predation on different varieties of raptorial birds by northern goshawks can continue unabated. Many types of <a href="/wiki/Owl" title="Owl">owl</a> are taken and in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, the northern goshawk is the second most prolific predator of owls behind the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_eagle_owl" class="mw-redirect" title="Eurasian eagle owl">Eurasian eagle owl</a> (<i>Bubo bubo</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">Bavaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, the 287&#160;g (10.1&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Long-eared_owl" title="Long-eared owl">long-eared owl</a> (<i>Asio otus</i>) was the second most common prey species for nesting goshawks.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bezzel_198-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bezzel-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Bia%C5%82owie%C5%BCa_Forest" title="Białowieża Forest">Białowieża Forest</a> of <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, fairly high numbers of the 475&#160;g (1.047&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Tawny_owl" title="Tawny owl">tawny owl</a> (<i>Strix alucco</i>) were taken.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_29-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Białowieża_151-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Białowieża-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> In all, some 18 species of owl have been recorded in the diet, ranging in size from the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_pygmy_owl" title="Eurasian pygmy owl">Eurasian</a> (<i>Glaucidium passerinum</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Northern_pygmy_owl" title="Northern pygmy owl">northern pygmy owls</a> (<i>Glaucidium gnoma</i>) at 58.5&#160;g (0.129&#160;lb) and 61.8&#160;g (2.18&#160;oz), respectively, to all the large northern <i><a href="/wiki/Strix_(genus)" title="Strix (genus)">Strix</a></i> owls including adults and even the 1,400&#160;g (3.1&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Great_horned_owl" title="Great horned owl">great horned owl</a>. Whether adults have ever been killed as prey though is unknown but goshawks have been known to kill great horned owls that they've found near their nests.<sup id="cite_ref-Rashid_54-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rashid-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Watson_121-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smithers2005_100-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smithers2005-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Drennan_161-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drennan-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_252-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> In addition, about eight species of <a href="/wiki/Falcon" title="Falcon">falcon</a> have been identified in the foods of goshawks. Adult falcons of small species such as <a href="/wiki/Kestrel" title="Kestrel">kestrels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Merlin_(bird)" title="Merlin (bird)">merlins</a> (<i>Falco columbarius</i>) can be overpowered quite easily if they can manage to surprise the prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Meng_119-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meng-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Petty_240-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petty-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hogan2010_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hogan2010-255">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> Larger falcons have turned up in the diet as well, including the 720&#160;g (1.59&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Prairie_falcon" title="Prairie falcon">prairie falcon</a> (<i>Falco mexicanus</i>) and the 966&#160;g (2.130&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Saker_falcon" title="Saker falcon">saker falcon</a> (<i>Falco cherrug</i>), although possible only nestlings of these species.<sup id="cite_ref-Drennan_161-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drennan-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-János_248-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-János-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> Brief aerial skirmishes between goshawks and <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine falcons</a> (<i>Falco peregrinus</i>) have been described but neither species is known to have killed one another in the wild.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup>  In <a href="/wiki/Schleswig-Holstein" title="Schleswig-Holstein">Schleswig-Holstein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, at least four small passerines species were recorded as nesting close to active goshawk nest, due to the incidental shelter that the fierce goshawks inadvertently provides from smaller raptors which are their main predators. Such raptors, including <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_kestrel" class="mw-redirect" title="Eurasian kestrel">Eurasian kestrels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_sparrowhawk" title="Eurasian sparrowhawk">Eurasian sparrowhawks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Long-eared_owl" title="Long-eared owl">long-eared owls</a>, not only avoid goshawk activity where possible but also were found to have lower nest productivity any time they nested relatively close to goshawks per the study.<sup id="cite_ref-Looft_232-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Looft-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> A similar phenomenon, with goshawks inadvertently providing shelter to small passerines, has been recorded in North America as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Rashid_54-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rashid-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Martes_americana_Yellowstone_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Martes_americana_Yellowstone_1.jpg/220px-Martes_americana_Yellowstone_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="280" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Martes_americana_Yellowstone_1.jpg/330px-Martes_americana_Yellowstone_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Martes_americana_Yellowstone_1.jpg/440px-Martes_americana_Yellowstone_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="816" data-file-height="1040" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Martes_americana_Yellowstone_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Prey selection frequently overlaps between goshawks and <a href="/wiki/American_marten" title="American marten">American martens</a>, seldom will both species prey on the other</div></div></div>
<p>Competition for northern goshawks can also come from <a href="/wiki/Carnivora" title="Carnivora">mammalian carnivores</a>. <a href="/wiki/Marten" title="Marten">Martens</a>, and to a lesser extent other <a href="/wiki/Weasel" title="Weasel">weasels</a>, are presumably one of their more major competitors as their diet often consists of similar prey primarily during spring and summer, <a href="/wiki/Tree_squirrel" title="Tree squirrel">tree squirrels</a> and woodland birds, but little has been studied in terms of how the two types of predator effect each other.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Most recorded interactions have been predatory, as the goshawk has been recorded preying on a dozen species, from the 122&#160;g (4.3&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Least_weasel" title="Least weasel">least weasel</a> (<i>Mustela nivalis</i>) to the 1,700&#160;g (3.7&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Stone_marten" class="mw-redirect" title="Stone marten">stone marten</a> (<i>Martes foina</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Belika_193-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Belika-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Verdal_114-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Verdal-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> Northern goshawks have also been recorded as feeding on much bigger predators such as the 5,775&#160;g (12.732&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Red_fox" title="Red fox">red fox</a> (<i>Vulpes vulpes</i>), the 4,040&#160;g (8.91&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Raccoon_dog" title="Raccoon dog">raccoon dog</a> (<i>Nyctereutes procyonoides</i>) and the 3,500&#160;g (7.7&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Striped_skunk" title="Striped skunk">striped skunk</a> (<i>Mephitis mephitis</i>), but it is not clear whether these were actual kills, as many may be encountered as already dead carrion.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_22-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Belika_193-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Belika-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> Domestic carnivores are sometimes eaten, including <a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">dogs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cat" title="Cat">cats</a>, the latter of which has reportedly been taken alive by goshawks.<sup id="cite_ref-Rashid_54-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rashid-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Drennan_161-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drennan-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> The red fox is a surprisingly considerable competitor for resources with northern goshawks. It was found in <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> that goshawk numbers were higher when <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">voles</a> were at peak numbers, not due to voles as a food source but because foxes were more likely to eat the rodents and ignore grouse, whereas during low vole numbers the foxes are more likely to compete with goshawks over grouse as prey.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> A decrease of the fox population of Norway due to <a href="/wiki/Mange" title="Mange">sarcoptic mange</a> was found result in an increase of grouse numbers and, in turn, northern goshawks.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup> In some areas, red foxes have been found to steal up to half of the goshawks’ kills.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike the predators at the top of the avian food chain such as <a href="/wiki/Eagle" title="Eagle">eagles</a> and the largest <a href="/wiki/Owl" title="Owl">owls</a>, which are rarely endangered by predation as adults, the northern goshawk is itself susceptible to a fairly extensive range of predators. The most deadly are likely to be the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_eagle_owl" class="mw-redirect" title="Eurasian eagle owl">Eurasian eagle owl</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Great_horned_owl" title="Great horned owl">great horned owl</a>, which not only predate goshawks of any age and at any season but also opportunistically take over their own prior nests as their own nesting site.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Of the two, the American horned owl nesting habits are more similar to goshawks, which most often consists of tree nests whereas the eagle owl usually nests in rock formations. Thus, the northern goshawk is more likely to victimized by the great horned owls, which can stage nightly ambushes and destroy an entire goshawk family as they pick off both adults and nestlings.<sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> In radio-tagging studies of adult and immature goshawks in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a> region and <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>, up to half of the studied birds were killed by great horned owls at night, while the horned owls accounted for 40% of the nest failures in studies from Arizona and <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Boal_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boal-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ward_266-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ward-266">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup> In comparison, in <a href="/wiki/Schleswig-Holstein" title="Schleswig-Holstein">Schleswig-Holstein</a>, 59% of reintroduced eagle owls used nests built by goshawks and no goshawk pairs could successfully nest within 500&#160;m (1,600&#160;ft) of an active eagle-owl nest. 18% of nest failures here positively were attributed to eagle owl predation, with another 8% likely due to eagle-owls.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup> Other larger raptorial birds can threaten them. The <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagle</a> (<i>Aquila chrysaetos</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Bald_eagle" title="Bald eagle">bald eagle</a> (<i>Haliaeetus leucocephalus</i>) in North America, have killed wintering goshawks, but given the discrepancy in their habitat preferences, such cases are presumably rare.<sup id="cite_ref-Squires_19-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Squires-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Other avian predators known to have successfully preyed on goshawks including adults (usually in singular cases) include <a href="/wiki/White-tailed_eagle" title="White-tailed eagle">white-tailed eagles</a> (<i>Haliaeetus albicilla</i>), <a href="/wiki/Eastern_imperial_eagle" title="Eastern imperial eagle">eastern imperial eagles</a> (<i>Aquila heliaca</i>), <a href="/wiki/Snowy_owl" title="Snowy owl">snowy owls</a> (<i>Bubo scandiacus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Ural_owl" title="Ural owl">Ural owls</a> (<i>Strix uralensis</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Red-tailed_hawk" title="Red-tailed hawk">red-tailed hawks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ponitz_227-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ponitz-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Moshkin_228-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moshkin-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The same mammalian predators that sometimes compete for food with northern goshawks also sometimes kill them, with the nestlings, fledglings and brooding females, all with impaired flight due to their wing feather moults, seemingly the most vulnerable. In one case, the <a href="/wiki/American_marten" title="American marten">American marten</a>, which at 660&#160;g (1.46&#160;lb) is the smallest marten and is sometimes taken as prey by the goshawks, successfully ambushed and preyed on a brooding female goshawk.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_121-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Chequamegon-Nicolet_National_Forest" class="mw-redirect" title="Chequamegon-Nicolet National Forest">Chequamegon-Nicolet National Forest</a> of <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>, the main source of mortality for northern goshawks is reintroduced <a href="/wiki/Fisher_(animal)" title="Fisher (animal)">fishers</a> (<i>Martes pennanti</i>), which are the largest kind of marten at 3,900&#160;g (8.6&#160;lb) and capably kill many chicks, fledglings and brooding females.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> In contrast, in Europe, the <a href="/wiki/Pine_marten" class="mw-redirect" title="Pine marten">pine marten</a> (<i>Martes martes</i>) has only been known to prey on young goshawks still in the nest and not adults.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup> Other mammals capable of climbing trees have been observed or inferred to predate goshawks, either mostly or entirely young in the nests, including <a href="/wiki/Wolverine" title="Wolverine">wolverines</a> (<i>Gulo gulo</i>), <a href="/wiki/North_American_porcupine" title="North American porcupine">North American porcupines</a> (<i>Erethizon dorsatum</i>), <a href="/wiki/Raccoon" title="Raccoon">raccoons</a> (<i>Procyon lotor</i>), <a href="/wiki/Bobcat" title="Bobcat">bobcats</a> (<i>Lynx rufus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/American_black_bear" title="American black bear">American black bears</a> (<i>Ursus americanus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McGowan_280-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McGowan-280">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Southwestern_281-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Southwestern-281">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup> Overall, the range of nest predators is more extensive in North America than in Eurasia, in the latter continent most recorded nest depredations are by eagle owls, with <a href="/wiki/Marten" title="Marten">martens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Corvid" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvid">corvids</a> usually only preying on goshawk nestlings when low food supplies cause the goshawks to have lower nest attendance (and presumably effect these predators to the extent that they take the risk of coming to the goshawk nest).<sup id="cite_ref-Rashid_54-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rashid-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Fledgling goshawks are also vulnerable to <a href="/wiki/Canid" class="mw-redirect" title="Canid">canids</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Coyote" title="Coyote">coyotes</a> (<i>Canis latrans</i>), <a href="/wiki/Gray_wolves" class="mw-redirect" title="Gray wolves">gray wolves</a> (<i>Canis lupus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Red_fox" title="Red fox">red foxes</a> as they may perch lower to the ground and are clumsier, more unsteady and less cautious than older birds.<sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Southwestern_281-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Southwestern-281">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup> In one case, a goshawk that was ambushed and killed at a kill by a mangy vixen fox was able to lethally slash the windpipe of the fox, which apparently died moments after partially consuming the goshawk.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Apart from aforementioned predation events, northern goshawks have at times been killed by non-predators, including prey that turned the tables on their pursuer, as well as in hunting accidents. In one case, a huge group (or murder) of <a href="/wiki/Hooded_crow" title="Hooded crow">hooded crows</a> heavily mobbed a goshawk that they caught in a relatively open spot, resulting in a prolonged attack that ended up killing the goshawk.<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup> In another instance, a goshawk drowned while attempting to capture a <a href="/wiki/Tufted_duck" title="Tufted duck">tufted duck</a> (Aythya fuligula).<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup> One young goshawk managed to escape a red fox that had caught it with a chewed wing, only to drown in a nearby creek.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Another, and rather gruesome, hunting mishap occurred when a goshawk caught a large mountain hare and, while attempting to hold it in place by grasping vegetation with its other foot, was torn in half.<sup id="cite_ref-Fischer_44-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Breeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_gentilis_MWNH_0762.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Accipiter_gentilis_MWNH_0762.JPG/170px-Accipiter_gentilis_MWNH_0762.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Accipiter_gentilis_MWNH_0762.JPG/255px-Accipiter_gentilis_MWNH_0762.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Accipiter_gentilis_MWNH_0762.JPG/340px-Accipiter_gentilis_MWNH_0762.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2365" data-file-height="2376" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_gentilis_MWNH_0762.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Egg Collection <a href="/wiki/Museum_Wiesbaden" title="Museum Wiesbaden">Museum Wiesbaden</a></div></div></div>
<p>The northern goshawk is one of the most extensively studied raptors in terms of its breeding habits. Adult goshawks return to their breeding grounds usually between March and April, but locally as early as February.<sup id="cite_ref-McGowan_280-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McGowan-280">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zirrer_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zirrer-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup> If prey levels remain high, adults may remain on their breeding ground all year.<sup id="cite_ref-Doyle_72-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doyle-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Arizona_244-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arizona-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> Courtship flights, calls and even nest building has been recorded in Finland exceptionally in September and October right after young dispersed, whereas in most of <a href="/wiki/Fennoscandia" title="Fennoscandia">Fennoscandia</a>, breeding does not commence any earlier than March and even then only when it is a warm spring.<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup> Most breeding activity occurs between April and July, exceptionally a month earlier or later.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Even in most areas of <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, most pairs have produced young by May.<sup id="cite_ref-McGowan_280-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McGowan-280">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup> Courtship flights typical are above the canopy on sunny, relatively windless days in early spring with the goshawks’ long main tail feathers held together and the undertail coverts spread so wide to give them an appearance of having a short, broad-tail with a long dark strip extending from the center.<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup> Display flights not infrequently escalate into an undulating flight, similar to a <a href="/wiki/Common_wood_pigeon" title="Common wood pigeon">wood pigeon</a> but with sharper turns and descents, and are sometimes embellished with sky-dives that can cover over 200&#160;m (660&#160;ft). One study found undulating display flights more than three times more often done by males than females.<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup> After display flights have concluded, the male typically brings a prepared fresh prey item to the female as part of the courtship. In general, these displays are presumably to show (or reinforce) to the potential mate their health and prowess as breeding partner.<sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schnell_34-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schnell-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Copulation is brief and frequent, ranging up to nearly 520 times per clutch (on average about 10 times a day or 100-300 throughout the season), and may be the male's way of ensuring paternity since he is frequently away gathering food by the time of egg-laying, although extra-pair copulation is extremely rare. Female solicits copulations by facing away from male with drooped wings and flared tail-coverts. The male, wings drooped and tail-coverts flared, drops from a branch to gain momentum, then swoops upward and mounts her back. Both birds usually call while mating.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup> Fidelity studies from Europe show that about 80–90% of adult females breed with the same male in consecutive years, whereas up to 96% of males mate with the same female in consecutive years.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, 72% of males retained relationship with the same mates in consecutive years while 70% of females did the same.<sup id="cite_ref-Detrich_292-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Detrich-292">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup> Males intruding in <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> territories were in some cases not evicted and ended up mating with the female, with the male of the pair not stopping it.<sup id="cite_ref-Rutz3_293-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rutz3-293">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> In migratory, northernmost populations, mate retention in consecutive years is low.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Detrich_292-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Detrich-292">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup> Males are sometimes killed by females during courtship and encounters can be dangerous especially if he does not bring food to courtship and he often seems nervous withdrawing with a trill at a given chance.<sup id="cite_ref-Kollinger_294-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kollinger-294">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nest_characteristics">Nest characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Nest characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hawks_nest.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Hawks_nest.jpg/220px-Hawks_nest.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="511" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Hawks_nest.jpg/330px-Hawks_nest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Hawks_nest.jpg/440px-Hawks_nest.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="3487" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hawks_nest.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nests are usually large structures placed quite high near the canopy on mature, tall trees, as seen on this <a href="/wiki/Birch" title="Birch">birch</a> in <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a></div></div></div>
<p>Nesting areas are indefinite, a nest may be used for several years, also a nest built years prior may be used or an entirely new nest may be constructed. When nest constructing, the pair will often roost together. Males construct most new nests but females may assist somewhat if reinforcing old nests. While the male is building, the female perches in the vicinity, occasionally screaming, sometimes flying to inspect the nest.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Holstein_295-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holstein-295">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup> At other times, the female may take a more active role, or even the primary one, in new nest construction and this is subject to considerable individual variation.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zirrer_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zirrer-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kollinger_294-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kollinger-294">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup> For the nesting tree, more than 20 species of <a href="/wiki/Conifer" class="mw-redirect" title="Conifer">conifer</a> have been used including <a href="/wiki/Spruce" title="Spruce">spruce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fir" title="Fir">fir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Larch" title="Larch">larch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pine" title="Pine">pine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tsuga" title="Tsuga">hemlock</a>. <a href="/wiki/Broadleaf_tree" class="mw-redirect" title="Broadleaf tree">Broadleaf trees</a> used including <a href="/wiki/Fraxinus" title="Fraxinus">ash</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alder" title="Alder">alder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aspen" title="Aspen">aspen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beech" title="Beech">beech</a>, <a href="/wiki/Birch" title="Birch">birch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elm" title="Elm">elm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hickory" title="Hickory">hickory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hornbeam" title="Hornbeam">hornbeam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tilia" title="Tilia">lime</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maple" title="Maple">maple</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Sycamore" title="Sycamore">sycamore</a>), <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Populus" title="Populus">poplar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tamarack" class="mw-redirect" title="Tamarack">tamarack</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prunus_serotina" title="Prunus serotina">wild cherry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Willow" title="Willow">willow</a>. In some areas, the nests may be lined with hard pieces of bark and also with green sprigs of conifers.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Often the tallest tree in a given stand is selected as the nest tree and this is often the dominant tree species within the given region and forest. Therefore, hardwood trees are usually used as the nesting tree in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_United_States" title="Eastern United States">eastern United States</a> while conifers are usually used in
