the <a href="/wiki/Western_United_States" title="Western United States">western United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Speiser_296-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Speiser-296">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup> Most nests are constructed under the canopy or near the main fork of a tree and in North America, averaging nest height ranged from 5.8&#160;m (19&#160;ft) (in the <a href="/wiki/Yukon" title="Yukon">Yukon</a>) to 16.9&#160;m (55&#160;ft) (in <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>), elsewhere as in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> average height is between 9 and 25&#160;m (30 and 82&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Doyle_72-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doyle-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup> In the dwarf trees of the tundra, nests have been found at only 1 to 2&#160;m (3.3 to 6.6&#160;ft) off the ground, and, in the tundra and elsewhere, very rarely on felled trees, stumps or on the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-Swem_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swem-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Englemann_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Englemann-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bent_299-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bent-299">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> In some studies from North America up to 15% of nests are in dead trees but this is far rarer in Eurasia.<sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup> More significant than species is the maturity and height of the nesting tree, its structure (which should have ample surface around the main fork) and, perhaps most significantly, little to no understory below it.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Penteriani,_V._2002_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penteriani,_V._2002-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Multiple studies note the habit of nests being built in forests close to clear-fellings, swamps and heaths, lakes and meadows, roads (especially light-use logging dirt roads), railways and swathes cut along power cables, usually near such openings there'd be prominent boulders, stones or roots of fallen trees or low branches to use as plucking points. Canopy cover averaged between 60 and 96% in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> As is typical in widely distributed raptors from temperate-zones, those from cold regions faced south, 65% in Alaska, 54% in Norway and also in high latitudes such as sky-forests of the Arizona Rockies, otherwise usually nests face north and east.<sup id="cite_ref-McGowan_280-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McGowan-280">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nests, especially after initial construction, may average between 80 and 120&#160;cm (31 and 47&#160;in) in length and50 to 70&#160;cm (20 to 28&#160;in) in width, and are around 20 to 25&#160;cm (7.9 to 9.8&#160;in) deep.<sup id="cite_ref-Bull_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bull-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McGowan_280-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McGowan-280">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bijlsma_304-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bijlsma-304">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup> After many uses, a nest can range up to 160&#160;cm (63&#160;in) across and 120&#160;cm (47&#160;in) in depth and can weigh up to a ton when wet.<sup id="cite_ref-Holstein_295-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holstein-295">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bijlsma_304-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bijlsma-304">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup> Northern goshawks may adopt nests of other species, <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">common buzzards</a> contributed 5% of nests used in Schleswig-Holstein, including unusually exposed ones on edges of woods and another 2% were built by <a href="/wiki/Common_raven" title="Common raven">common ravens</a> or <a href="/wiki/Carrion_crow" title="Carrion crow">carrion crows</a>, but 93% were built by the goshawks themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-Looft_232-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Looft-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> While colonizing peri-urban areas in Europe, they may displace Eurasian sparrowhawks not only from their territories but may actually try to use overly small sparrowhawk nests, usually resulting in nest collapse.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> One nest was used continuously by different pairs for a period of 17 years.<sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup> A single pair may maintain up to several nests, usually up to two will occur in an area of no more than a few hundred kilometers. One nest may be used in sequential years, but often an alternate is selected. During an 18-year-study from Germany, many alternate nests were used, 27 pairs had two, 10 had 3, 5 had 4, one had five and one pair had as many as 11. Other regions where pairs had on average two nests were <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>’s <a href="/wiki/Kaibab_Plateau" title="Kaibab Plateau">Kaibab Plateau</a>. The extent of use of alternate nests is unknown as well as their benefit, but they may reduce significant levels of parasites and diseases within the nest.<sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Link_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Link-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Arizona_244-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arizona-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-306" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-306">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup> In central Europe, the goshawk's nest area can be as small 1 to 2&#160;ha (2.5 to 4.9 acres) of woods and less than 10 hectares are commonplace. Usually only 1 active nest occurs per 100&#160;ha (250 acres), are they avoid edges as nest sites and occupied nests are seldom less than 600&#160;m (2,000&#160;ft) apart.<sup id="cite_ref-Looft_232-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Looft-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bijlsma_304-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bijlsma-304">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup> The most closely spaced active nests by a separate pair on record was 400&#160;m (1,300&#160;ft) in central Europe, another case of two active nests 200&#160;m (660&#160;ft) apart in Germany was a possible case of polygamy.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eggs">Eggs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Eggs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The eggs are laid at 2- to 3-day intervals on average between April and June (usually May), taking up to 9 days for a clutch of 3–4 and 11 days for a clutch of 5.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> The eggs are rough, unmarked pale bluish or dirty white.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In <i>A. g, atricapillus</i>, the average dimensions of the eggs are reported at 57.76 to 59.2&#160;mm (2.274 to 2.331&#160;in) in height by 44.7 to 45.1&#160;mm (1.76 to 1.78&#160;in) in width, with ranges of  52–66&#160;mm (2.0–2.6&#160;in) x 42–48&#160;mm (1.7–1.9&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Palmer_41-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palmer-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bent_299-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bent-299">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup> In Spanish eggs, the average dimensions were 56.3&#160;mm ×&#160;43&#160;mm (2.22&#160;in ×&#160;1.69&#160;in) compared to  German ones, which averaged 57.3&#160;mm ×&#160;44&#160;mm (2.26&#160;in ×&#160;1.73&#160;in). Goshawks from <a href="/wiki/Lapland_(Finland)" title="Lapland (Finland)">Lapland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> lay the  largest known eggs at 62–65&#160;mm (2.4–2.6&#160;in) x 47–49.5&#160;mm (1.85–1.95&#160;in), while other Finnish goshawk eggs ranged from 59–64&#160;mm (2.3–2.5&#160;in) x 45–48&#160;mm (1.8–1.9&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Weight of the eggs average 59&#160;g (2.1&#160;oz) in America, 63&#160;g (2.2&#160;oz) in Great Britain and 50 to 60&#160;g (1.8 to 2.1&#160;oz) in Poland and Germany, with extreme weights from the latter nations of 35 to 75&#160;g (1.2 to 2.6&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_22-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-310" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-310">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup> Clutch size almost always averages between 2 and 4 eggs, with a median around 3, rarely as few as 1 or as many 5–6 will be laid.<sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Huhtala,_K._1981_311-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Huhtala,_K._1981-311">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup> In combination spring weather and prey population levels seem to drive both egg laying dates and clutch size.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> If an entire clutch is lost, a replacement can be laid within 15 to 30 days.<sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_58-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parental_behavior">Parental behavior</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Parental behavior">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Northern_Goshawk_on_Nest._Accipiter_gentilis_-_Flickr_-_gailhampshire.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Northern_Goshawk_on_Nest._Accipiter_gentilis_-_Flickr_-_gailhampshire.jpg/220px-Northern_Goshawk_on_Nest._Accipiter_gentilis_-_Flickr_-_gailhampshire.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="194" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Northern_Goshawk_on_Nest._Accipiter_gentilis_-_Flickr_-_gailhampshire.jpg/330px-Northern_Goshawk_on_Nest._Accipiter_gentilis_-_Flickr_-_gailhampshire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Northern_Goshawk_on_Nest._Accipiter_gentilis_-_Flickr_-_gailhampshire.jpg/440px-Northern_Goshawk_on_Nest._Accipiter_gentilis_-_Flickr_-_gailhampshire.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2622" data-file-height="2310" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Northern_Goshawk_on_Nest._Accipiter_gentilis_-_Flickr_-_gailhampshire.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mother goshawk seldom leaves the nest in either the incubation or the brooding stage, until the young are about 2 weeks</div></div></div>
<p>During incubation, females tend to become quieter and more inconspicuous. The mother can develop a brooding patch of up to 15 by 5&#160;cm (5.9 by 2.0&#160;in) on her underside. She may turn the eggs as frequently as every 30 to 60 minutes. Males may incubate as many as 1 to 3 hours, but usually less than an hour, early in incubation but rarely do so later on. During daylight females can do as much as 96% of the observed incubation. The incubation stage last for any time between 28 and 37 days (rarely up to 41 days in exceptionally big clutches), varying in different parts of the range.<sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_58-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Beebe_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beebe-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wight_231-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wight-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> After hatching occurs, the male does not come directly to the nest but instead just delivers food (usually already plucked, beheaded or otherwise dismembered) to a branch near the nest which the female tears apart and shares between herself and the nestlings.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Holstein_295-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holstein-295">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup> Food deliveries by the male can be daily or as infrequent as every 3 to 5 days. In turn, the female must feed the young about twice a day in order for the chicks to avoid starvation. Caching of food has been recorded near the nest, but only before the young start feeding themselves. Food deliveries must average about 250 to 320&#160;g (8.8 to 11.3&#160;oz) per young goshawk per day for them to successfully fledge, or 700 to 950&#160;g (1.54 to 2.09&#160;lb) total daily and 60 to 100&#160;kg (130 to 220&#160;lb) throughout the season for an average sized clutch of around three. Females will also start capturing prey later on, but usually only after the young have already fledged.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schnell_34-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schnell-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rutz_98-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rutz-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, female goshawks may press down on their nest if a human approaches, others may unobtrusively leave the nest, although are more reluctant to leave the nest late in incubation. In <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, the behavior of parent goshawks differs, as they often vigorously defend their territories fiercely from all intruders, including passing humans. The northern goshawk has a reputation as the most aggressive American raptor when the vicinity of their nest is approached. Here, when the nest is approached (especially soon after hatching) the goshawk will engage in their defensive kakking vocal display accompanied by exaggerated swooping in flight which quickly phases into a violent attack, potentially causing painful (but usually minor) injuries and blood loss. Research has indicated that attacks on humans are mostly done by adult females (more than 80% of the time) and are rarely pressed unless a person is by themselves. However, large groups and loud noise can appear to irritate the female and may cause her to attack the next lone person who comes near the nest. The higher aggression towards humans in North America than in Europe has been linked to both a more extensive range of potential nest predators for American goshawks causing them to develop a more aggressive display or the lower rates of persecution in America than in Europe, which may account for the relative shyness in the latter continent.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rashid_54-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rashid-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Speiser_296-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Speiser-296">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">&#91;313&#93;</a></sup> Occasionally, both males and females have been recorded abandoning the nest and their mates. There are a few rare cases where males successfully reared up to 4 young after the female abandoned the nest or was killed between the 2 and 3rd week. Otherwise male will continue delivering prey but without the female all the nestlings will starve to death and the food simply rots.<sup id="cite_ref-Looft_232-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Looft-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">&#91;314&#93;</a></sup> In cases where the male abandons the female and the brood, she may be able to successfully brood but usually only one nestling is likely to survive to fledge without the male's contribution of prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Rashid_54-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rashid-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> At other times the mother may be replaced, sometimes forcefully, by another female, usually an older mature one.<sup id="cite_ref-Looft_232-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Looft-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup> Exceptional cases of polygamy, with a male mating with two females, have been reported in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Netherlands" class="mw-redirect" title="The Netherlands">The Netherlands</a> and typically these breeding attempts fail.<sup id="cite_ref-Bijlsma_304-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bijlsma-304">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rutz3_293-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rutz3-293">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hatching_and_development">Hatching and development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Hatching and development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Acc_nest.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Acc_nest.jpg/220px-Acc_nest.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Acc_nest.jpg/330px-Acc_nest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Acc_nest.jpg/440px-Acc_nest.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Acc_nest.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nestling northern goshawks in Germany</div></div></div>
<p>Hatching is asynchronous but not completely, usually an average sized clutch takes only 2 to 3 days to hatch, although it may take up to 6 days to hatch a clutch of more than 4 eggs.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_58-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Hatchlings start calling from within the shell as much as 38 hours before hatching, as a faint <i>chep, chep, chack, peep, peep, peep</i> may be heard.<sup id="cite_ref-Fischer_44-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> The young are covered with down and <a href="/wiki/Altricial" class="mw-redirect" title="Altricial">altricial</a> (as are all raptors) at first but develop rapidly. Hatchlings measure about 13&#160;cm (5.1&#160;in) long at first and grow about 5 to 9&#160;cm (2.0 to 3.5&#160;in) in length each week until they fledge. The mothers typically brood the nestlings intensively for about two weeks, around the time grayer feathers start to develop through the nestlings’ down. The most key time for development may be at three weeks when the nestlings can stand a bit and start to develop their flight feathers. Also at the three-week stage, they can reach about half the adults’ weight and females start to noticeably outgrow the males. However, this growth requires increased food delivery so frequently results in lower nest attendance and, in turn, higher predation rates. Also rates of starvation at this stage can exceed 50% especially in the youngest of large clutches of 4 to 5.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rashid_54-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rashid-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Siewert_316-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Siewert-316">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Boal2_317-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boal2-317">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup> Nestlings at 4 weeks are starting to develop strong flight feathers, which they frequently flap; also they can start to pull on food but are still mainly fed by female and begin to make a whistling scream when she goes to fetch food from the male. More active feeding behavior by nestlings may increase their aggression towards each other. By the 5th week, they've developed many typical goshawk behaviors, sometimes mantling over food, testing balance by extending one leg and one wing at edge of nest (called "warbling" by falconers) and can wag their tails vigorously. Starvation risk also increases at this point due to their growing demands and, due to their incessant begging calls, vocal activity may court predators. In 6th week, they become "branchers", although still spend much of the time by the nest, especially by the edge. The young goshawks "play" by seizing and striking violent at a perch or by yanking off leaves and tossing them over their back. Wing feathers do not develop highly dimorphically, but male branchers are better developed than females who have more growing to do and can leave the nest up to 1–3 days sooner. The young rarely return to the nest after being 35 to 46 days of age and start their first flight another 10 days later, thus becoming full fledglings.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Siewert_316-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Siewert-316">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Boal2_317-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boal2-317">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup> Goshawk nestlings frequently engage in “runting”, wherein the older siblings push aside and call more loudly and are thus are feed more often at food deliveries, until the younger siblings may either starve to death, be trampled or killed by their siblings (referred to as <a href="/wiki/Siblicide" title="Siblicide">siblicide</a> or “cainism”). There is some evidence that mother goshawks may lessen the effects of runting by delaying incubation until their last eggs are laid. Food supply may be linked to higher rates of siblicides and, in many locations with consistent prey levels, runting and siblicide can occur somewhat seldomly (meaning the northern goshawk is a “facultative” rather than “obligate cainist”).<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Siewert_316-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Siewert-316">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Segarra_318-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Segarra-318">&#91;318&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-319">&#91;319&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320">&#91;320&#93;</a></sup> Nonetheless, either by predation, starvation or siblicide, few nests produce more than 2 to 3 fledglings. One pair in North America was able to successfully fledge all four of its young.<sup id="cite_ref-Siewert_316-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Siewert-316">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">&#91;321&#93;</a></sup> Somewhat larger numbers of female fledglings are produced in Europe with their larger size, but the opposite is true in North America where sexual dimorphism is less pronounced. When food supplies are very high, though, European goshawks actually can produce somewhat more males than females.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-322" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-322">&#91;322&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_O%C3%B6logist_for_the_student_of_birds,_their_nests_and_eggs_(1911)_(14562268417).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/The_O%C3%B6logist_for_the_student_of_birds%2C_their_nests_and_eggs_%281911%29_%2814562268417%29.jpg/220px-The_O%C3%B6logist_for_the_student_of_birds%2C_their_nests_and_eggs_%281911%29_%2814562268417%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="315" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/The_O%C3%B6logist_for_the_student_of_birds%2C_their_nests_and_eggs_%281911%29_%2814562268417%29.jpg/330px-The_O%C3%B6logist_for_the_student_of_birds%2C_their_nests_and_eggs_%281911%29_%2814562268417%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/The_O%C3%B6logist_for_the_student_of_birds%2C_their_nests_and_eggs_%281911%29_%2814562268417%29.jpg/440px-The_O%C3%B6logist_for_the_student_of_birds%2C_their_nests_and_eggs_%281911%29_%2814562268417%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1402" data-file-height="2006" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_O%C3%B6logist_for_the_student_of_birds,_their_nests_and_eggs_(1911)_(14562268417).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two juveniles from <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a> after they've become "branchers", or have left the nest but are not yet flying competently</div></div></div>
<p>At about 50 days old, the young goshawks may start hunting on their own but more often eat carrion either provided by parents or biologists. Most fledglings stay within 300&#160;m (980&#160;ft) of the nest at 65 days of age but can wander up to 1,000&#160;km (620&#160;mi) before dispersal at between 65–80 days old in sync with the full development of their flight feathers. Between 65 and 90 days after hatching, more or less all young goshawks become independent. There is no evidence that parents aggressively displace the young in the fall (as other raptorial birds have sometimes been reported to do), therefore the young birds seek independence on their own.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rashid_54-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rashid-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Goshawk siblings are not cohesive together past 65 days, except for some lingering young females, whereas <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">common buzzard</a> broods are not recorded at their nests after 65 days but remain strongly cohesive with each other.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup> 5% of radio-tagged young in <a href="/wiki/Gotland" title="Gotland">Gotland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> (entirely males) were found to disperse to another breeding area and join a different brood as soon as their flight feathers were developed enough. These seem to be cases of moving to a better food area. Parents and adoptive young seem to tolerate this, although parents do not seem to be able to tell the difference between their own and other young.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> It is only after dispersal that goshawks typically start to hunt and seem to drink more often than older birds, sometimes spend up to an hour bathing.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding_success_rates">Breeding success rates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Breeding success rates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Nest success averages between 80 and 95% in terms of the number of nests that produce fledglings, with an average number of 2 to 3 fledglings per nest. About equal numbers of eggs and nestlings may be lost (6% lost in each the incubation and brooding periods per a study from <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>) but according to a study from <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> large clutches of 4 to 5 had higher losses overall than medium-sized clutches of 2 to nearly 4. Total losses averaged 36% in Spain across clutches of 2–5. Similar results were found in Germany, with similar numbers of fledglings produced in very large clutches (more than 4) as in medium-sized ones (2–4).<sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Arizona_244-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arizona-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Looft_232-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Looft-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Segarra_318-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Segarra-318">&#91;318&#93;</a></sup> A grading of success from a study in <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> found categories of competent and less competent pairs, with losses averaging 7% and 17% in these two groups, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Wikman_324-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wikman-324">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup> Studies from <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Yukon_Territory" class="mw-redirect" title="Yukon Territory">Yukon Territory</a> found that average number of fledglings varied dramatically based on food supply based on the cyclical nature of most prey in these northern areas, varying from average success rates of 0 to 3.9 fledglings in the latter region. Similar wide variations in breeding success in correlation to prey levels were noted at other areas, including <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a> (where the number of fledglings could be up to seven times higher when lagomorphs were at their population peaks) and <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_3-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Doyle_72-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doyle-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">&#91;325&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-326">&#91;326&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nesting_failure">Nesting failure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Nesting failure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Poor weather, which consists of cold springs that bear late cold spells, snow, and freezing rain, causes many nests to fail, and may also hamper courtship and lower brood size and overall breeding attempts.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-327" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-327">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup> However, the most important cause of nest failure was found to be nest destruction by humans and other predations, starvation, then bad weather and collapse of nests in declining order.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wikman_324-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wikman-324">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup> On average, humans are responsible based on known studies for about 17% of nest failures in Europe. 32% of 97 nestlings in <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">Bavaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> died because of human activities, while 59% of 111 broods in England failed due to this factor.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Link_17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Link-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-328" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-328">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup> Low food supplies are linked to predation, as it seems to cause greater risk of predation due to the lower nest attendance.<sup id="cite_ref-Link_17-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Link-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Lower densities of pairs may actually increase nesting success, as per studies from <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> where the highest median clutch size, at 3.8, was in the area with the lowest densities.<sup id="cite_ref-Huhtala,_K._1981_311-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Huhtala,_K._1981-311">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, in <a href="/wiki/Schleswig-Holstein" title="Schleswig-Holstein">Schleswig-Holstein</a>, nest failure was 14% higher where active nests were closer than 2&#160;km (1.2&#160;mi) apart compared to nests farther than this.<sup id="cite_ref-Looft_232-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Looft-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> Age may also play a factor in nest success, pairings where one mate is not fully mature (usually the female, as males rarely breed before attaining adult plumage) is less than half as successful as ones where both were mature, based on studies from Arizona.<sup id="cite_ref-Arizona_244-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arizona-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wight_231-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wight-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> Overall, males do not normally breed at any younger than 3 years of age (although they are in adult plumage by two years) and females can breed at as young as 1 to 2 years old, but rarely produce successful, viable clutches. The age at sexual maturity is the same as other northern <i>Accipiters</i> as well as most buteonine hawks (eagles, on the other hand, can take twice as long to attain full sexual maturity).<sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> 6–9 years of age seem to be the overall peak reproductive years for most northern goshawks. However, some females can reproduce at as old as 17 years old and <a href="/wiki/Senescence" title="Senescence">senescence</a> is ambiguous in both sexes (possibly not occurring in males).<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Median values of brood success was found to be 77% in Europe and 82% in North America overall. Conversely, the median brood size is about half a chick smaller in North America than in Europe. In Europe, clutch size overall averages 3.3, the number of nestlings averages 2.5 and fledglings averages 1.9.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marquiss_144-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marquiss-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Lifespan">Lifespan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Lifespan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Djan-l%27-blanc_sol_dos.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Djan-l%27-blanc_sol_dos.jpg/220px-Djan-l%27-blanc_sol_dos.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Djan-l%27-blanc_sol_dos.jpg/330px-Djan-l%27-blanc_sol_dos.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Djan-l%27-blanc_sol_dos.jpg/440px-Djan-l%27-blanc_sol_dos.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Djan-l%27-blanc_sol_dos.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Goshawks may be killed by collisions with man-made objects</div></div></div>
<p>The lifespan in the wild is variable. It is known that in captivity, northern goshawks may live up to 27 years of age.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Wild birds that survive their first two years can expect a lifespan of up to 11 years.<sup id="cite_ref-329" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-329">&#91;329&#93;</a></sup> There is one record (apparently sourced to the <a href="/wiki/American_Ornithologists%27_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="American Ornithologists&#39; Union">AOU</a>) of a 16-year, 4-month-old goshawk.<sup id="cite_ref-pauldfrost_330-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pauldfrost-330">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Fennoscandia" title="Fennoscandia">Fennoscandia</a>, starvation was found to account for 3-6% of reported deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-331" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-331">&#91;331&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, 9% of deaths were from starvation, but the percentage of demises from this increased to the north and affected juveniles more so than adults.<sup id="cite_ref-332" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-332">&#91;332&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Gotland" title="Gotland">Gotland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, 28% of mortality was from starvation and disease.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Both bacterial and viral diseases have been known to cause mortality in wild northern goshawks.<sup id="cite_ref-Ward_266-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ward-266">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-333" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-333">&#91;333&#93;</a></sup> Variable numbers of goshawks are killed by flying into man-made objects such as power lines and buildings and by automobiles, although lesser numbers are affected by powerline collisions than larger types of raptor.<sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marquiss_144-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marquiss-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Status">Status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The breeding range of the northern goshawk extends over one-third of North America and Asia each and perhaps five-sixths of Europe, a total area of over 30,000,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (12,000,000&#160;sq&#160;mi). Densities in western and central Europe were recorded at 3–5 pairs per 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi). In boreal Sweden, numbers vary from 1 to 4.5 pairs per 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi), while in similar habitat in Alaska there were 0.3 to 2.7/100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi). An average of only 1 pair per 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi) would give world population of 600,000 breeding birds, likely at least half as many immature and other non-breeders. Recent study found 145,000–160,000 in Europe or 1 pair/60&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (23&#160;sq&#160;mi) to 1 pair/54&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (21&#160;sq&#160;mi). The total population of northern goshawks in the world probably ranges well over a million.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The total European populations, estimated at as many as 160,000 pairs, makes it the fourth most numerous raptor in the continent, after the <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">common buzzards</a> (&gt;700,000 pairs), <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_sparrowhawk" title="Eurasian sparrowhawk">Eurasian sparrowhawk</a> (&gt;340,000 pairs) and <a href="/wiki/Common_kestrel" title="Common kestrel">common kestrel</a> (&gt;330,000 pairs). The most populated countries by goshawks in Europe were <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> (an estimated 10,000 pairs), <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> (8,500 pairs), <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> (6,000 pairs) and <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> (5,600 pairs). The highest densities of breeding pairs per 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi) of land were in <a href="/wiki/The_Netherlands" class="mw-redirect" title="The Netherlands">The Netherlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, although this is biased due to the small land area of these countries.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-334" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-334">&#91;334&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> has a roughly estimated 85,000 pairs of northern goshawk.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, there are a broadly estimated 150,000–300,000 individuals. In North America, most western populations at mid-latitudes have approximately 3.6–10.7 pairs100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi). A total of 107 nesting territories (1991–1996) were located on a 1,754&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (677&#160;sq&#160;mi) study area on the Kaibab Plateau, AZ, resulting in a density of 8.4 pairs/100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi). The estimated density in Pennsylvania (1.17 pairs/100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi)) suggests that eastern populations may occur at lower densities than western populations, but densities of eastern populations may increase as these populations recover. Typically, populations at far northern latitudes may occur at lower densities than those of southwestern and western populations in North America.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-335" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-335">&#91;335&#93;</a></sup> Although median densities was similar, populations are overall much denser in Europe than in North America. The hotspots of density for goshawks in Europe lie in east-central Europe (around <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>) and in west-central area (the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>/<a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West Germany</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-336" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-336">&#91;336&#93;</a></sup> Per the <a href="/wiki/IUCN" class="mw-redirect" title="IUCN">IUCN</a>, the global population is estimated to consist of 1 million to nearly 2.5 million birds, making this one of the most numerous species in its diverse family (the <a href="/wiki/Red-tailed_hawk" title="Red-tailed hawk">red-tailed hawk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Black_kite" title="Black kite">black kite</a> both may have a similar global population size, whereas the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_sparrowhawk" title="Eurasian sparrowhawk">Eurasian sparrowhawk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">common buzzard</a> are possibly slightly more numerous than goshawks despite their smaller ranges).<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-337" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-337">&#91;337&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-338" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-338">&#91;338&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-339" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-339">&#91;339&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Mortality rates for first-year goshawks is often considerably higher than older birds. In studies from <a href="/wiki/Gotland" title="Gotland">Gotland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Schleswig-Holstein" title="Schleswig-Holstein">Schleswig-Holstein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, 40–42% of first-years died. By the second year, mortality rates drop to 31–35%, based on ring studies from the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>. Based on studies from <a href="/wiki/Gotland" title="Gotland">Gotland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Southwestern_United_States" title="Southwestern United States">southwestern United States</a>, annual mortality for adults is 15–21%, however, feather results indicate that annual mortality for adult goshawks is up to 7% higher in Europe than in North America.<sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hoglund_69-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hoglund-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bijlsma_304-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bijlsma-304">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-340" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-340">&#91;340&#93;</a></sup> In many parts of the range, especially Europe, historic populations decreased regionally due to human persecution (especially shooting), disturbance and epidemic loss of habitat, especially during the 19th century and early 20th.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Some states, like Pennsylvania, paid $5 bounties on Goshawks in the 1930s.<sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> From 1880–1930, an estimated 3,000–5,500 goshawk were being killed annually in Norway when bounties were offered. Shooting rate lowered later, causing the average number of goshawks shot to drop to 654 to for the period 1965–1970.<sup id="cite_ref-341" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-341">&#91;341&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-342" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-342">&#91;342&#93;</a></sup> Northern goshawks continue to be persecuted in Norway, shown by the high turnover rate of breeding females in Telemark County, revealed by DNA analysis of moulted feathers.<sup id="cite_ref-343" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-343">&#91;343&#93;</a></sup>   In Finland, where the species was not legally protected, 4,000–8,000 goshawks were being killed annually from 1964–1975.<sup id="cite_ref-344" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-344">&#91;344&#93;</a></sup> Most goshawks shot are incautious juveniles, with 58% of juvenile mortality in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> and 59% from the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> being killings by humans. Increase of pheasant releases in <a href="/wiki/Vendsyssel" title="Vendsyssel">Vendsyssel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> from 6,000 to 35,000 since 1994 has resulted in fewer goshawks as they often hunt the pheasants in winter and are shot, legally, by the region's gamekeepers.<sup id="cite_ref-345" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-345">&#91;345&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-346" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-346">&#91;346&#93;</a></sup> As recently as about 5 years before that, intentional killing by humans continued as the main cause of mortality for goshawks on <a href="/wiki/Gotland" title="Gotland">Gotland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, causing 36% of deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-347" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-347">&#91;347&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, the northern goshawk was <a href="/wiki/Local_extinction" title="Local extinction">extirpated</a> in the 19th century because of specimen collectors and persecution by <a href="/wiki/Gamekeeper" class="mw-redirect" title="Gamekeeper">gamekeepers</a>, but in recent years it has come back by immigration from <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, escaped <a href="/wiki/Falconry" title="Falconry">falconry</a> birds, and deliberate releases. The goshawk is now found in considerable numbers in <a href="/wiki/Kielder_Forest" title="Kielder Forest">Kielder Forest</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northumberland" title="Northumberland">Northumberland</a>, which is the largest forest in Britain. Overall there are some 200 birds in Great Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kenward_9-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenward-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Morrison_348-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morrison-348">&#91;348&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jastrz%C4%85b_mlody.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Jastrz%C4%85b_mlody.jpg/220px-Jastrz%C4%85b_mlody.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Jastrz%C4%85b_mlody.jpg/330px-Jastrz%C4%85b_mlody.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Jastrz%C4%85b_mlody.jpg/440px-Jastrz%C4%85b_mlody.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2500" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jastrz%C4%85b_mlody.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Juvenile goshawk from <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the 1950s–1960s declines were increasingly linked with <a href="/wiki/Pesticide_pollution" class="mw-redirect" title="Pesticide pollution">pesticide pollution</a>. However, in early 1970s pesticide levels in the United States for goshawks were low. Eggshell thinning has not been a problem for most populations, although <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> eggshells (weight and thickness index) pre-1947 (pre-DDT) to 1947—1964 (DDT in use) declined some 8-12%. In <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, migratory goshawks during the winter of 1972-1973 invasion year contained less organochlorine and PCB residues than did other raptors, however, these birds were probably from nonagricultural, northern forests.<sup id="cite_ref-349" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-349">&#91;349&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-350" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-350">&#91;350&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-351" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-351">&#91;351&#93;</a></sup> Higher DDT levels seemed to have persisted quite recently in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>. This was the case in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, especially in former East Germany where DDT was widely available until 1988, having been largely discontinued elsewhere after the 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-352" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-352">&#91;352&#93;</a></sup> Goshawks, which had increased in The Netherlands after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> due to less persecution, new woodlands and increased pigeon numbers, were found to have suddenly crashed from the late 1950s on. It was later revealed that this was due to DDT, the number of breeding pairs decreasing 84% from 1958 to 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-353" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-353">&#91;353&#93;</a></sup> As opposed to DDT, the main contaminant found to have reduced goshawks in Scandinavia during the 20th century were <a href="/wiki/Methylmercury" title="Methylmercury">methyl mercury seed dressings</a> used to reduce fungal attack in livestock.<sup id="cite_ref-354" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-354">&#91;354&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_gentilis_-owned_by_a_falconer_in_Scotland_-upper_body-8a.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Accipiter_gentilis_-owned_by_a_falconer_in_Scotland_-upper_body-8a.jpg/220px-Accipiter_gentilis_-owned_by_a_falconer_in_Scotland_-upper_body-8a.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Accipiter_gentilis_-owned_by_a_falconer_in_Scotland_-upper_body-8a.jpg/330px-Accipiter_gentilis_-owned_by_a_falconer_in_Scotland_-upper_body-8a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Accipiter_gentilis_-owned_by_a_falconer_in_Scotland_-upper_body-8a.jpg/440px-Accipiter_gentilis_-owned_by_a_falconer_in_Scotland_-upper_body-8a.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="801" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_gentilis_-owned_by_a_falconer_in_Scotland_-upper_body-8a.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Falconer's bird in Scotland</div></div></div>
<p>Seemingly the remaining persistent conservation threat to goshawks, given their seeming overall resilience (at the species level) to both persecution and pesticides, is deforestation. Timber harvests are known to destroy many nests and adversely regional populations.<sup id="cite_ref-Deforestation_230-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deforestation-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-355" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-355">&#91;355&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-356" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-356">&#91;356&#93;</a></sup> Harvest methods that create extensive areas of reduced forest canopy cover, dropping to cover less than 35-40%, may be especially detrimental as cases of this usually cause all goshawks to disappear from the area.<sup id="cite_ref-Bright-Smith_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bright-Smith-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-357" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-357">&#91;357&#93;</a></sup> However, the mortality rates due to foresting practices are unknown and it is possible that some mature goshawks may simply be able to shift to other regions when a habitat becomes unsuitable but this is presumably unsustainable in the long-term.<sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_8-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> In harvest forests of <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, where overstory trees are frequently removed, goshawks have been found to successfully remain as breeding species as long as some mature stands are left intact. Despite the decline of habitat quality and the frequent disturbances, this region's goshawks breeding success rates somewhat improbably did not reduce.<sup id="cite_ref-358" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-358">&#91;358&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-359" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-359">&#91;359&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, a study from <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> and <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> shows that goshawks only left woodlots when the canopy was reduced by more than 30%, although the European goshawk populations have long been known to be adaptable to some degree of habitat fragmentation.<sup id="cite_ref-360" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-360">&#91;360&#93;</a></sup>  Based on habitat usage studied in <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>, this adaptability is not seen everywhere, as here nests were further from human habitation than expected on the basis of available habitat, an observation suggesting that disturbance regionally can reduce habitat quality.<sup id="cite_ref-361" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-361">&#91;361&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, studies from the <a href="/wiki/American_southwest" class="mw-redirect" title="American southwest">American southwest</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> have indicated that heavily logged areas caused strong long-term regional declines for goshawks. In <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>, it was found that even when the nests were left intact, the noisy timber harvest work often caused failure of nesting during the incubation stage, and all nesting attempts that were occurring within 50 to 100&#160;m (160 to 330&#160;ft) of active logging failed, frequently after parents abandoned the nest.<sup id="cite_ref-Boal2_317-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boal2-317">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-362" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-362">&#91;362&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-363" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-363">&#91;363&#93;</a></sup> Other noisy activity, such as camping, have also caused nests to failure.<sup id="cite_ref-Speiser2_364-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Speiser2-364">&#91;364&#93;</a></sup> Wildlife researchers and biologists do not seem to negatively affect goshawk nests, as they aware to keep forays to the nest brief and capture of adult goshawks for radio-tagging was found to not harm their success at raising broods.<sup id="cite_ref-Speiser2_364-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Speiser2-364">&#91;364&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In North America, several non-governmental conservation organizations petitioned the Department of Interior, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Fish_%26_Wildlife_Service" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Fish &amp; Wildlife Service">United States Fish &amp; Wildlife Service</a> (1991 &amp; 1997) to list the goshawk as "threatened" or "endangered" under the authority of the <a href="/wiki/Endangered_Species_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Endangered Species Act">Endangered Species Act</a>. Both petitions argued for listing primarily on the basis of historic and ongoing nesting habitat loss, specifically the loss of old-growth and mature forest stands throughout the goshawk's known range. In both instances, the U.S. Fish &amp; Wildlife Service concluded that listing was not warranted, but state and federal natural resource agencies responded during the petition process with standardized and long-term goshawk inventory and monitoring efforts, especially throughout U.S. Forest Service lands in the Western U.S. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Forest_Service" title="United States Forest Service">United States Forest Service</a> (US Dept of Agriculture) has listed the goshawk as a "sensitive species", while it also benefits from various protection at the state level. In North America, the goshawk is federally protected under the <a href="/wiki/Migratory_Bird_Treaty_Act_of_1918" title="Migratory Bird Treaty Act of 1918">Migratory Bird Treaty Act of 1918</a> by an amendment incorporating native birds of prey into the Act in 1972. The northern goshawk is also listed in Appendix II of the Convention on International Trade in Endangered Species (<a href="/wiki/CITES" title="CITES">CITES</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Woodbridge2006_365-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Woodbridge2006-365">&#91;365&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_humans">Relationship with humans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Relationship with humans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Antoin_Sevruguin_8_A_falconer.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Antoin_Sevruguin_8_A_falconer.jpg/220px-Antoin_Sevruguin_8_A_falconer.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Antoin_Sevruguin_8_A_falconer.jpg/330px-Antoin_Sevruguin_8_A_falconer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Antoin_Sevruguin_8_A_falconer.jpg/440px-Antoin_Sevruguin_8_A_falconer.jpg 2x" data-file-width="496" data-file-height="677" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Antoin_Sevruguin_8_A_falconer.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Iranian falconer with a trained goshawk</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_culture">Human culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Human culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The northern goshawk appears on the <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_the_Azores" title="Flag of the Azores">flag</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms_of_the_Azores" title="Coat of arms of the Azores">coat of arms</a> of the Azores. The archipelago of the <a href="/wiki/Azores" title="Azores">Azores</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>, takes its name from the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese language</a> word for goshawk, (<i>açor</i>), because the explorers who discovered the archipelago thought the birds of prey they saw there were goshawks; later it was found that these birds were <a href="/wiki/Milvus" title="Milvus">kites</a> or <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">common buzzards</a> (<i>Buteo buteo rothschildi</i>). The goshawk features in <a href="/wiki/Stirling" title="Stirling">Stirling</a> Council's coat of arms via the crest of the <a href="/wiki/Clan_Drummond" title="Clan Drummond">Drummond Clan</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_falconry">In falconry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: In falconry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The name "goshawk" is a traditional name from <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon language">Anglo-Saxon</a> <i>gōshafoc</i>, literally "goose hawk".<sup id="cite_ref-ODBBN_366-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODBBN-366">&#91;366&#93;</a></sup> The name implies prowess against larger quarry such as wild geese, but were also flown against crane species and other large waterbirds. The name "goose hawk" is somewhat of a misnomer, however, as the traditional quarry for goshawks in ancient and contemporary <a href="/wiki/Falconry" title="Falconry">falconry</a> has been <a href="/wiki/Rabbit" title="Rabbit">rabbits</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pheasant" title="Pheasant">pheasants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Partridge" title="Partridge">partridge</a>, and medium-sized <a href="/wiki/Waterfowl" class="mw-redirect" title="Waterfowl">waterfowl</a>, which are similar to much of the prey the species hunts in the wild. A notable exception is in records of traditional <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japanese</a> falconry, where goshawks were used more regularly on goose and <a href="/wiki/Crane_(bird)" title="Crane (bird)">crane</a> species.<sup id="cite_ref-Jameson1962_367-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jameson1962-367">&#91;367&#93;</a></sup> In ancient European falconry literature, goshawks were often referred to as a <a href="/wiki/Yeoman" title="Yeoman">yeoman</a>'s bird or the "cook's bird" because of their utility as a hunting partner catching edible prey, as opposed to the <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine falcon</a>, also a prized falconry bird, but more associated with noblemen and less adapted to a variety of hunting techniques and prey types found in wooded areas. The northern goshawk has remained equal to the <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine falcon</a> in its stature and popularity in modern <a href="/wiki/Falconry" title="Falconry">falconry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-F.L._Beebe,_H.M._Webster_2000_368-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-F.L._Beebe,_H.M._Webster_2000-368">&#91;368&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-369" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-369">&#91;369&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Goshawk hunting flights in falconry typically begin from the falconer's gloved hand, where the fleeing bird or rabbit is pursued in a horizontal chase. The goshawk's flight in pursuit of prey is characterized by an intense burst of speed often followed by a binding maneuver, where the goshawk, if the prey is a bird, inverts and seizes the prey from below. The goshawk, like other <a href="/wiki/Accipiter" title="Accipiter">accipiters</a>, shows a marked willingness to follow prey into thick vegetation, even pursuing prey on foot through brush.<sup id="cite_ref-F.L._Beebe,_H.M._Webster_2000_368-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-F.L._Beebe,_H.M._Webster_2000-368">&#91;368&#93;</a></sup> Goshawks trained for falconry not infrequently escape their handlers and, extrapolated from the present day British population which is composed mostly of escaped birds as such, have reasonably high survival rates, although many do die shortly after escape and many do not successfully breed.<sup id="cite_ref-370" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-370">&#91;370&#93;</a></sup> The effect of modern-day collection of northern goshawks for falconry purposes is unclear, unlike some falcon species which can show regional declines due to heavy falconry collections but can increase in other areas due to established escapees from falconers.<sup id="cite_ref-371" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-371">&#91;371&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-372" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-372">&#91;372&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_goshawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
